And it is better for the sharh to mention it to complete his aim. His saying: 'at its time' – the time for performing Ramadan is after sunset until just before forenoon, so any part of it, I found sahih. And for its make-up, the whole night, and intention after dawn is not valid. His saying: 'contradicts the validity of his act' – it is apparent to delete 'validity'. His saying: 'from menstruation and postnatal bleeding' – so freedom from them is among conditions of obligation, i.e., obligation of performance, and conditions of validity. His saying: 'because they contradict it' – it is better to add 'it'. His saying: 'by its ways upon him' is attached to 'invalidates it'. His saying: 'due to his ability to remove it' meaning unlike menstruation and postnatal bleeding. His saying: 'and necessity of its occurrence' meaning and due to necessity of its occurrence, i.e., a person may be compelled to it at night and the day may come suddenly upon him, meaning dawn appears upon him without being able to perform ghusl. And the intent is not restriction to necessity; rather the meaning is that this may happen, so the Lawgiver did not consider it.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah, explaining the conditions for the obligation and validity of fasting in the Hanafi school. The author lists four conditions for obligation: knowledge of the obligation (for a new Muslim), health, freedom from menstruation/postnatal bleeding, and residency. Knowledge is obtained through testimony-like reports; there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa requires 'adalah (justice), while the Sahibayn do not, and the mufta bihi is that even a single just person suffices. Health is a condition due to the Quranic concession for the ill. Freedom from menses and postnatal bleeding is a condition for obligation (they are not obligated to fast) and for validity (if they occur during fast, it breaks). Residency conditions trip for travelers. For validity, three conditions: intention (must be made each day before dawn for Ramadan, can be made any part of the night for make-up), freedom from nullifiers such as menstrual/postnatal bleeding (which invalidate), and freedom from things that spoil the fast. Janaba (major impurity) is not a condition for validity because one can purify oneself before dawn, though it is advisable to avoid. The commentary elaborates on the definition of 'just' and details of knowledge transmission.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A just person who is free from major sins and does not persist in minor sins; defined as one against whom no accusation is made regarding stomach or private parts, i.e., honest and chaste.
نية (niyyah): Intention to fast, which must be made each day for Ramadan, and its time is from sunset to just before forenoon (for Ramadan) or the whole night for make-up fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the validity of fasting for someone who becomes junub during the night and does not perform ghusl until after dawn?
A) The fast is invalid and must be made up.
B) The fast is valid because janaba is not a condition for validity.
C) The fast is valid only if the person intended to perform ghusl before dawn.
D) The fast is invalid if the person had the ability to purify but delayed intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan is obligatory only when four conditions are met; validity requires three conditions, with the key point that janaba does not invalidate, and the knowledge condition for new Muslims is subject to a lenient view (mufta bihi) that does not require the reporter to be just.

Sawm — Fasting

Rukn, Hukm, and Wisdom of Fasting
Defines rukn, hukm, and wisdom of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rukn of fasting, explain its hukm (including the case of prohibited fasts), and articulate the wisdom behind its legislation.

Core Idea
The rukn is abstaining; the hukm combines duty discharge and reward; wisdom tames the soul and fosters sympathy.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi defines the essential pillar of fasting, its legal effect, and its inner purpose. Understanding these components is foundational for fiqh of sawm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rukn and its scope
The passage states that the rukn of fasting is al-kaf (abstaining) from the two desires (stomach and private parts) and what is annexed to them. Identify what constitutes the rukn and note that it is a single act of internal restraint.
The rukn is not just physical abstinence but includes restraint from all intakes and actions that break the fast.
2
Understand the hukm: discharge of duty and reward
The passage explains that the ruling (hukm) of a valid fast is the dropping of the duty (sugut al-wajib) from the liability and the attainment of reward as a grace from Allah. Distinguish between obligatory (fard) and other types of fasts.
The hukm applies whether the fast is fard, wajib, or nafl, but for nafl the duty is self-imposed.
3
Analyze the exception of prohibited fasts
The passage clarifies that if a fast is prohibited (e.g., on Eid al-Nahr), it is still valid (sahih) in the sense that the rukn is fulfilled and the duty is discharged, but the person is sinful for rejecting Allah's hospitality. The commentary analogizes to prayer in usurped land.
This shows that validity (sihhah) and permissibility (ibahah) are separate; a valid act can be sinful due to external factors.
4
Reflect on the wisdom (hikmah)
The passage then discusses the wisdom of fasting: it calms the soul that commands evil, turns it away from excesses, purifies the heart, and fosters sympathy for the poor. Understand that these are underlying benefits, not legal causes.
Hikmah is not part of the ruling but explains the purpose of the legislation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And residence is among conditions of validity, for if insanity occurs and remains until sunset, his fast is valid. And its rukn (pillar) i.e., of fasting, is al-kaf, i.e., abstaining from fulfilling the two desires: of the stomach and the private parts, and what is annexed to them, which we will mention. Its ruling is the dropping of the duty, i.e., the necessary, whether fard or other, from the liability, by the obligation of Allah or the servant, and the reward, as a grace from Allah, in the Hereafter, if it is not prohibited. If it is prohibited, like the fast of al-Nahr, its ruling is validity and exit from responsibility, and sin by turning away from the hospitality of Allah. And the wisdom of the legislation of fasting includes: that the soul, which commands to evil, is calmed by turning away from excesses, because when it hungers, all limbs are satisfied, and when it is satisfied, the limbs hunger, meaning they become strong in striking, looking, and doing what is not proper. By their restraint, the heart becomes clear and watchfulness is attained. And also includes sympathy for the poor by feeling their pain. Commentary: His saying: 'and residence is among conditions of validity' – for if insanity occurs and remains until sunset, his fast is valid. His saying: 'and its rukn is al-kaf' i.e., abstaining. His saying: 'and what is annexed to them' like medicine. His saying: 'and its ruling' i.e., of fasting as such. His saying: 'or the servant' and its obligation by his vow or beginning, and this is regarding a wajib fast or a nafl? His saying: 'as a grace from Allah' i.e., the reward is a grace from Allah, not by way of obligation, nor by way of necessity. His saying: 'and sin by turning away from the hospitality of Allah' – in it, sin from one direction does not negate the attainment of reward from another direction, which is the meaning of what the author of al-Nahr said: that prohibition due to an adjacent meaning does not negate reward, like prayer in usurped land. His saying: 'and the wisdom of legislation of fasting' – it is better to add 'many'. His saying: 'calming of the soul' i.e., from moving in what displeases. His saying: 'which commands to evil' i.e., to evil, and his saying 'by turning away' is attached to 'calming' and the ba' is for causality. His saying: 'from excesses' i.e., from the extra matters that concern the obligated person occurring from the limbs. His saying: 'all limbs are satisfied' i.e., they refrain from moving in what displeases. If you say that hunger prevents them from moving in acts of obedience as well, the answer is that the intended hunger is not excessive hunger leading to that. His saying: 'from their movements' i.e., evil movements. His saying: 'meaning they become strong' – the intended meaning of hunger here is desire, so by this interpretation, the suspicion that hunger implies restraint is repelled. His saying: 'and doing what is not proper' is an appositive to the general. His saying: 'by their restraint the heart becomes clear' – for the cause of its turbidity is the excesses of the limbs; when they are restrained from them, it becomes clear and thereby one attains high ranks, as in.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage defines the essential pillar (rukn) of fasting and its legal ruling, followed by a discussion of its wisdom. The rukn of fasting is abstinence (imsak) from fulfilling the two desires: of the stomach (eating and drinking) and the private parts (sexual intercourse), as well as anything that is analogous, such as medicine. The hukm (ruling) of a valid fast is that it discharges the obligatory duty (whether fard or wajib) from the liability, and the fasting person receives reward from Allah as a grace. However, if the fast is of a type that is prohibited, such as fasting on the day of Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah), it is valid in form (sahih) in the sense that it fulfills the act of fasting, but the person is sinning by rejecting Allah's hospitality on that day. The commentary clarifies that sin and reward can coexist when the prohibition is due to an external factor, similar to prayer in usurped land. The wisdom of fasting includes: (1) it subdues the soul that commands to evil by making it turn away from excesses; when the soul is hungry, the limbs are restrained from improper actions, the heart becomes clear, and one attains mindfulness; (2) it fosters sympathy for the poor by experiencing hunger. The commentary refutes the notion that extreme hunger could prevent good deeds, explaining that the intended hunger is moderate.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): Pillar of fasting; the essential act of abstaining (imsak) from the two desires and their annexes.
حكمة (hikmah): Wisdom behind the legislation of fasting; includes spiritual and social benefits such as taming the soul and sympathy for the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a person who fasts on the day of al-Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah)?
A) The fast is invalid and must be made up.
B) The fast is valid but he is sinful for rejecting Allah's hospitality.
C) The fast is valid and he receives reward because he intended to fast.
D) The fast is invalid because it is prohibited, and he earns sin.

Key Takeaway
The rukn of fasting is abstinence; its hukm combines discharge of duty and reward, with the nuance that prohibited fasts are valid but sinful; and its wisdom includes spiritual purification and social empathy.

The Pillar and Conditions of Validity of Fasting
Definition of the pillar and conditions of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillar (rukn) of fasting in the Hanafi school and explain which conditions affect its validity.

Core Idea
The pillar of fasting is abstention from the two appetites; conditions like sanity are required initially but loss after intention does not invalidate.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the fundamental elements of fasting: its pillar (rukn) and the conditions for its validity. Special attention is given to the effect of insanity on the fast—a nuanced ruling that distinguishes Hanafi fiqh from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rukn
Find the statement that defines the essential part of fasting. The text explicitly says 'Its pillar is the refrain (al-kaff)'. Note that al-kaff means abstention from the two appetites (food/drink and sexual desire) and what is annexed to them.
The term 'rukn' in Hanafi fiqh means an integral part without which the act is void.
2
Understand the conditions
The text mentions 'residing' (iqamah) as a condition of validity. Also, sanity is required at the time of intention. However, if insanity occurs after intention and persists until sunset, the fast is valid.
This is a key difference from schools that require sanity throughout the day.
3
Analyze the insanity case
Work through the reasoning: The condition of sanity is for the initial intention. Once the intention is made, the essential abstention can continue even without sanity, so the fast remains valid.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Note what is annexed
The text says 'and from what is annexed to them'. This includes things like medication inserted into the body that break the fast? The annexed items will be detailed later but conceptually they are treated like the two appetites.
In Hanafi fiqh, anything that reaches the stomach or is absorbed breaks the fast.
5
Summarize the overall ruling on validity
Based on the rukn and conditions, a valid fast requires intention at dawn, and then abstention from the two appetites and their annexes until sunset. Conditions like residence and initial sanity apply.
There is no requirement to be free from insanity for the entire day.

Book of Fasting. Residing is among the conditions of validity, for if insanity occurs and remains until sunset, his fast is valid. Its pillar (i.e., fasting) is the refrain (i.e., abstention from fulfilling the two appetites (of the belly and the private parts) and from what is annexed to them, as will be mentioned). Its ruling is the dropping of the obligatory (i.e., what is required, whether fard or other) from the liability (by the obligation of God or the servant) and the reward (as a favor from God) in the hereafter, unless it is prohibited, like the fast of sacrifice; then its ruling is validity, discharge of responsibility, and sin due to turning away from God's hospitality. Among the wisdom of the legislation of fasting: it quiets the soul that incites to evil by turning away from excess, because when the limbs are filled, the hand, foot, eye, and other limbs refrain from their movements. When the stomach is full, all limbs are full; when the soul is full, the limbs hunger, meaning they become strong to strike, look, and do what is not proper. By their restraint, the heart becomes clear and vigilance is attained. Among it is compassion for the poor by feeling the pain of hunger. [Commentary: Explaining various terms and additional points, including that the prohibited fast of sacrifice is valid but sinful, and that the reward is a favor from God. The commentary also discusses the meaning of 'soul that incites to evil', 'excess', and the wisdom of fasting including acquiring the attribute of angels and the exclusion of hypocrisy in fasting.] On the description of fasting and its division: Fasting is divided into six types, mentioned summarily then in detail. [Continues with commentary on vigilance, compassion, and the hadith that God says 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it' excluding others, and the division into six types with eight in detail because the obligatory is either specific, which is Ramadan performance, or non-specific.]
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage is a hashya (super-commentary) on Maraqi al-Falah dealing with the foundational rulings of fasting. The author first addresses the conditions of validity: residence (iqamah) is a condition, and insanity that occurs after intention and persists until sunset does not invalidate the fast. The pillar (rukn) of fasting is defined as abstention (al-kaff) from the two appetites—food and drink (belly) and sexual desire (private parts), as well as anything that is legally treated like them (e.g., medication). The ruling (hukm) of valid fasting is that it discharges the obligation from the person's liability, both for obligations imposed by God (like Ramadan) and by the servant (like a vow), and it brings reward from God as a favor. However, if the fast is prohibited (e.g., the fast of the Day of Sacrifice), it is still valid and discharges the obligation, but it incurs sin for rejecting God's hospitality. The author then lists the wisdom (hikmah) behind fasting: it suppresses the inciting soul (nafs ammarah) by turning away from excess, leading to the limbs refraining from sinful movements, purifying the heart, and fostering God-consciousness (muraqabah). Another wisdom is empathy for the poor by experiencing hunger. The passage then transitions to the classification of fasting, stating that it is divided into six types, which will be detailed later. The commentary clarifies that these types include obligatory, required, and other categories, with the obligatory being either specific (Ramadan) or non-specific. The text also discusses the hadith 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it' to explain that hypocrisy (riya') does not enter fasting in the same way as other acts, though this point is refined: for obligatory acts, riya' does not nullify the act's obligation-scrapping function, but it does remove the reward of excellence.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Fasting: refraining from the two appetites (food/drink and sexual desire) from dawn to sunset with intention.
الركن (al-rukn): Pillar: the essential part of fasting, which is the abstention (al-kaff) from the two appetites.
الكف (al-kaff): Refrain: the act of abstaining from fulfilling the two appetites.
شهوتي البطن والفرج (shahwatay al-batn wa al-farj): The two appetites: hunger/thirst and sexual desire.
الحكمة (al-hikmah): Wisdom: the underlying reasons for legislation, including subduing the soul and empathy.
المراقبة (al-muraqabah): Vigilance: maintaining awareness of God's commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Intention (niyyah)
B) Abstention from the two appetites (al-kaff)
C) Staying away from all sins
D) Reading the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The pillar of fasting is abstention from the two appetites; loss of sanity after intention does not break the fast.

Sawm — Fasting

The Ruling and Wisdom of Fasting
Ruling and wisdom of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling (hukm) of valid fasting and list the wisdoms (hikam) behind its legislation as per the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Valid fasting discharges obligation and brings reward; prohibited fasts are valid but sinful. Its wisdoms include subduing the soul and fostering empathy.

This passage continues with the legal effects of fasting: what it achieves (discharge of duty, reward) and the exceptional case when it is prohibited. It then delves into the philosophical purposes (hikam) of fasting, including spiritual purification and social solidarity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic ruling
The text states: 'Its ruling is the dropping of the obligatory from the liability and the reward.' This means a valid fast fulfills the requirement (whether fard or wajib) and earns reward from God as a favor.
The obligation can be from God (Ramadan) or from the servant (vow, nafl started).
2
Note the exception for prohibited fasts
If the fast is prohibited (e.g., Day of Sacrifice), it is still valid (sahih) and discharges the obligation, but the person commits a sin for turning away from God's hospitality.
This shows that validity (sihha) and sin can coexist in the same act.
3
Explore the wisdom of subduing the soul
The text says fasting quiets the inciting soul (nafs ammarah) by turning away from excess. When the stomach is full, the limbs become active in sin; when hungry, the limbs are restrained, the heart becomes clear, and God-consciousness (muraqabah) increases.
This is a spiritual benefit: taming the lower self.
4
Recognize the social wisdom
Another wisdom is empathy for the poor: by feeling hunger, the rich are moved to compassion and charity.
This connects worship to social ethics.
5
Reflect on the hadith about riya'
The hadith 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it' indicates that hypocrisy (riya') does not enter fasting as easily as other acts. However, the commentary clarifies that for obligatory acts, riya' does not nullify the obligation-scrapping function; it only removes the reward of excellence.
This nuance is important for understanding sincerity in fasting.

Book of Fasting. Residing is among the conditions of validity, for if insanity occurs and remains until sunset, his fast is valid. Its pillar (i.e., fasting) is the refrain (i.e., abstention from fulfilling the two appetites (of the belly and the private parts) and from what is annexed to them, as will be mentioned). Its ruling is the dropping of the obligatory (i.e., what is required, whether fard or other) from the liability (by the obligation of God or the servant) and the reward (as a favor from God) in the hereafter, unless it is prohibited, like the fast of sacrifice; then its ruling is validity, discharge of responsibility, and sin due to turning away from God's hospitality. Among the wisdom of the legislation of fasting: it quiets the soul that incites to evil by turning away from excess, because when the limbs are filled, the hand, foot, eye, and other limbs refrain from their movements. When the stomach is full, all limbs are full; when the soul is full, the limbs hunger, meaning they become strong to strike, look, and do what is not proper. By their restraint, the heart becomes clear and vigilance is attained. Among it is compassion for the poor by feeling the pain of hunger. Commentary
This passage is a hashya (super-commentary) on Maraqi al-Falah dealing with the foundational rulings of fasting. The author first addresses the conditions of validity: residence (iqamah) is a condition, and insanity that occurs after intention and persists until sunset does not invalidate the fast. The pillar (rukn) of fasting is defined as abstention (al-kaff) from the two appetites—food and drink (belly) and sexual desire (private parts), as well as anything that is legally treated like them (e.g., medication). The ruling (hukm) of valid fasting is that it discharges the obligation from the person's liability, both for obligations imposed by God (like Ramadan) and by the servant (like a vow), and it brings reward from God as a favor. However, if the fast is prohibited (e.g., the fast of the Day of Sacrifice), it is still valid and discharges the obligation, but it incurs sin for rejecting God's hospitality. The author then lists the wisdom (hikmah) behind fasting: it suppresses the inciting soul (nafs ammarah) by turning away from excess, leading to the limbs refraining from sinful movements, purifying the heart, and fostering God-consciousness (muraqabah). Another wisdom is empathy for the poor by experiencing hunger. The passage then transitions to the classification of fasting, stating that it is divided into six types, which will be detailed later. The commentary clarifies that these types include obligatory, required, and other categories, with the obligatory being either specific (Ramadan) or non-specific. The text also discusses the hadith 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it' to explain that hypocrisy (riya') does not enter fasting in the same way as other acts, though this point is refined: for obligatory acts, riya' does not nullify the act's obligation-scrapping function, but it does remove the reward of excellence.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Fasting: refraining from the two appetites (food/drink and sexual desire) from dawn to sunset with intention.
الحكمة (al-hikmah): Wisdom: the underlying reasons for legislation, including subduing the soul and empathy.
المراقبة (al-muraqabah): Vigilance: maintaining awareness of God's commands and prohibitions.
الرياء (al-riya'): Hypocrisy: performing acts of worship to be seen by others rather than for God.
النفس الأمارة (al-nafs al-ammarah): The soul that incites to evil; the lower self prone to sinful desires.
سقوط الواجب (suqut al-wajib): Discharge of the obligation; the requirement is considered fulfilled.

Sawm — Fasting

Classification of Fasting into Six Types
Commentary on classification and rulings of fasting types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six types of fasting and distinguish their ruling levels (fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Fasting is divided into six types; the ruling level varies—Ramadan is fard ayn, expiations are fard in practice, and vowed fasts are fard in the most apparent view.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah on Maraqi al-Falah provides a detailed breakdown of the six categories of fasting, clarifying the precise legal status of each type. It also explores the wisdom behind fasting, the unique protection from riya in fasting based on a hadith qudsi, and the distinction between fard in belief and fard in practice. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly identifying the obligation level of any fast one might undertake or prescribe.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six types of fasting
The text states that fasting is divided into six types: fard (obligatory), wajib (required but not definitive), sunnah (recommended), mandub (preferred), nafl (supererogatory), and makruh (disliked). The commentary expands that in detail there are eight, because fard includes specific (Ramadan in time) and non-specific (qada), and wajib includes specific (vowed) and non-specific (absolute vow).
The six types are the standard Hanafi classification for acts of worship.
2
Distinguish 'fard in belief' from 'fard in practice'
The text explains that expiatory fasts (kaffarat) are fard in practice (amalan) but not in belief (i'tiqadan). Denying them does not make one a disbeliever, because consensus on their obligation is not definitive. This is crucial for understanding the spectrum of obligation in Hanafi fiqh.
This distinction applies only to kaffarat and vowed fasts; Ramadan fasts are fard in both senses.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference on vowed fasts (nadhr)
For a nadhr (vowed) fast, the most apparent view (al-azhar) is that it is fard (practical), but some scholars say it is wajib because the evidence is zanni (non-definitive). The text notes both views are preponderant (mutarajjihan).
This difference does not affect the practical ruling: both views consider the vowed fast binding.
4
Analyze the special status of fasting regarding riya
The hadith qudsi 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it' indicates that riya does not enter into fasting as it does in other acts. However, the commentator adds that all obligatory acts are free from riya in fulfilling the obligation, but voluntary aspects can be tainted. This nuance helps reconcile the hadith with general principles.
The key takeaway: fasting is specially protected from riya, but this does not mean other obligations are automatically riya-prone.
5
Summarize the thirteen types of necessary fasting and consecutiveness
The tanbih at the end lists thirteen types of necessary fasting: seven require consecutiveness (including Ramadan, kaffarat, and specific vows) and six do not (including qada and some expiations). This detail is vital for practical application.
The list integrates all the fard and wajib types discussed earlier.

الشرح قوله: (وتحصل (المراقبة أي المحافظة على أوامر الله تعالى ونواهيه. قوله: (ومنها العطف على المساكين قال في الشرح : فإن الصائم لما ذاق ألم الجوع في بعض الأوقات تذكر في عموم الأوقات فيسارع إليه بالرقة والرحمة وحقيقتها في حق الإنسان نوع ألم باطني، فينال بذلك ما عند الله تعالى من حسن الجزاء، ومنها موافقته الفقراء بتحمل ما يتحملون أحياناً، وفي ذلك رفع حاله عند الله . قوله : (لمن هو وصفه أبداً) اللام بمعنى على، ومصدوق من المساكين، والأولى حذفه للإستغناء عنه بقوله على المساكين. قوله: (ولذا) أي لما ذكر من الحكم قوله : ( في السحور) بالضم الفعل أي الأكل. قوله: (والاتصاف) بالرفع عطف على قوله العطف، وهو صريح ما في الشرح قوله : (بصفة الملائكة) فإنهم لا يأكلون، ولا يشربون وهم متلبسون بالعبادة قوله : (ولا يدخل الرياء في صوم ! وفي سائر الطاعات يدخل لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ويقول الله تعالى الصوم لي وأنا أجزي به نفى شركة الغير وهذا لم يذكر في سائر الطاعات كذا في الشرح ، وفيه أن الفرائض كلها لا رياء فيها قال في الدر قبيل باب صفة الصلاة ولا رياء في الفرائض في حق إسقاط الواجب، وكذا ذكره آخر الحظر، فلا خصوصية للصوم أما إذا كان أحسنها بين الناس، وكان بحيث لو كان في الخلوة لا يحسن فليس له ثواب الإحسان، ثم الحديث عام للصوم الفرض والنفل لأن إمساكه في خلوته إنما هو الله تعالى، ، وقيل في معنى الحديث : إن الحسنات تؤخذ في المظالم إلا الصوم وقيل : أنه لم يعبد به غيره، وقيل: غير ذلك، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
في صفة الصوم وتقسيمه ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام أي إجمالاً، وبالتفصيل هي ثمانية لأنّ الفرض أما معين وهو صوم رمضان أداء أو غير معين، وهو صومه قضاء والواجب كذلك فالمعين كالنذر المعين وغير المعين كالنذر المطلق أفاده في الدر. قوله : (ذكرت) أي الأقسام مجملة أي لم يبين فيها الأفراد، ثم مفصلة ببيان أفرادها قوله : ( لكونه أوقع في النفس أي لكون التفصيل المفهوم من قوله مفصلة، وذلك لذكره بعد الاشتياق إلى البيان قوله : (وصوم (الكفارات لكنه فرض . عملاً لا اعتقاداً، ولذا لا يكفر جاحده در . قوله: (الظهار ) أي كفارة الظهار الخ وقوله : والقتل أي الخطأ، ومثله كفارة الإفطار، وإنما لم يذكرها لأنها مثلها، وأما صوم المتعة والقرآن فليس من صوم الكفارات، وإن كان فرضاً فسقط ما في السيد قوله : وفدية الأذى) كما إذا حلق، أو لبس بعذر فإنه يخير بين الذبح والإطعام والصيام فإذا اختار الصوم كان فرضاً. قوله: الثبوت هذه بالقاطع) عله لكونها فرضاً إلا أن الإجماع لم ينعقد على فرضية الكفارات فلذا كان عملياً فيها كما في سكب الأنهر، والقاطع هو القرآن فالظهار في المجادلة، والقتل في النساء، واليمين في المائدة وكذا جزاء الصيد وفدية الأذى في البقرة في قوله تعالى : فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو به أذى من رأسه) [البقرة: ٢] الآية. قوله : (سنداً) أي رجالاً، والمراد بقطعية السند أن رجاله ثقات وبقطعية المتن أي اللفظ أنه لم ينسخ بغيره، ولم يعارضه ما هو أقوى منه مما يدل عليه. قوله: (والإجماع عليها قد علمت ما ذكره في سكب الأنهر من أنّ الإجماع لم ينعقد على فرضية الكفارات حتى عد صاحب الملتقى صوم الكفارات من الواجب قوله : (فهو فرض في الأظهر) أي فرض عملي لأنّ مطلق الإجماع لا يفيد الفرض القطعي در، وقيل أنه واجب لأنه خص من آية وليوفوا نذورهم النذر بما ليس من جنسه واجب كعيادة المريض، فلم يبق قطعياً، وصار كخير الواحد، وبمثله يثبت الوجوب لا الفرض كذا في الشرح والحاصل أن القولين مرجحان .
تنبيه : الصوم اللازم ثلاثة عشر قسماً سبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع وهي رمضان، وكفارة القتل، وكفارة اليمين وكفارة الظهار وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين وغير المعين إذا التزم فيه التتابع ، أو نواه إلا أن صوم كفارة القتل والظهار والإفطار واليمين والنذر المطلق إذا ذكر فيه التتابع أو نواه إذا أفطر في خلاله استقبله واستأنفه وصوم رمضان، والنذر المعين لا يلزم فيهما الاستئناف بقطع التتابع، وستة لا يجب فيها التتابع وهي قضاء رمضان وصوم كفارة الحلق وصوم جزاء الصيد وصوم النذر المطلق عن ذكر التتابع ، أو نيته وصوم وصوم اليمين بأن قال: والله لأصومن شهراً هذا محصل ما في شرح السيد.
Book of Fasting. Hunger is for the one for whom it is a permanent attribute, so he does good to him (the poor). Therefore, one should not overeat at suhoor because it prevents the intended wisdom, and the attribute of the angels. And riya (show-off) does not enter into the fasting of the obligatory (fast). Regarding the description of fasting and its division: Fasting is divided into six types: mentioned briefly then in detail. This is its state from... The commentary says: 'And al-muraqabah (vigilance) is obtained' meaning observance of Allah's commands and prohibitions. His saying: 'And among them is kindness to the poor'—the commentator said: For the fasting person, when he tastes the pain of hunger sometimes, he remembers it at all times, so he hastens to tenderness and mercy, and its reality in terms of a human is a type of internal pain, thus he attains thereby the good reward from Allah. And among them is his agreement with the poor by bearing what they bear sometimes, and in that is raising his status with Allah. His saying: 'For whom it is a permanent attribute'—the lam is in the sense of 'upon', and the predicate is 'the poor', and it is better to omit it because it is dispensed with by his saying 'upon the poor'. His saying: 'Therefore'—i.e., because of the mentioned wisdom. His saying: 'in al-suhur'—with dammah on the first letter, meaning the action of eating. His saying: 'And the attribute'—in the nominative case, coordinated with his saying 'kindness', and it is explicit in the commentary. His saying: 'with the attribute of the angels'—for they do not eat nor drink, and they are engaged in worship. His saying: 'And riya does not enter into fasting! And into other acts of obedience it does enter, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: Allah says: Fasting is for Me and I reward it, denying partnership of others. And this was not mentioned in other acts of obedience, as in the commentary. And in it that all obligations are free from riya. He said in al-Durr before the chapter on the description of prayer: 'And there is no riya in obligations regarding the fulfillment of the obligatory.' Likewise he mentioned it at the end of al-Hazr, so there is no particularity for fasting. But if he beautifies it among people, and if he were in private he would not beautify it, then he has no reward for beautification. Then the hadith is general for obligatory and voluntary fasting, because his abstinence in private is for Allah. And it was said regarding the meaning of the hadith: Good deeds are taken for oppressions except fasting. And it was said: No one else was worshipped by it. And it was said: other than that. And Allah knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah, the Almighty. Regarding the description of fasting and its division: Fasting is divided into six types, i.e., in summary, and in detail they are eight, because obligatory (fard) is either specific, i.e., fasting of Ramadan in time, or non-specific, i.e., making it up. And obligatory (wajib) is likewise: the specific like a specific vow, and the non-specific like an absolute vow. He said: 'They were mentioned'—i.e., the types briefly, without detailing their individuals, then in detail by explaining their individuals. His saying: 'Because it is more impactful on the soul'—i.e., because the detail understood from his saying 'detailed', and that is because it was mentioned after yearning for explanation. His saying: 'And fasting of expiations (kaffarat)'—but it is fard in practice, not in belief, so the one who denies it does not become a disbeliever. His saying: 'al-dhihar'—i.e., expiation of dhihar, etc. And his saying: 'al-qatl'—i.e., killing (by mistake), and similarly the expiation of breaking the fast, but he did not mention it because it is similar. As for fasting of tamattu' and qiran, it is not from expiatory fasting, although it is fard. His saying: 'And fidyat al-adha'—as when he shaves or wears (due to excuse), he is given a choice between slaughter, feeding, and fasting. If he chooses fasting, it becomes obligatory. His saying: 'Established by definitive evidence'—the reason for it being fard, except that consensus was not reached on the obligation of expiations, so it was practical therein. The definitive evidence is the Quran: al-dhihar in al-Mujadilah, killing in al-Nisa', the oath in al-Ma'idah, and likewise the penalty for hunting and fidyah of harm in al-Baqarah. His saying: 'sanadan'—i.e., transmitters, meaning by definitive transmission that its narrators are trustworthy, and by definitive text that the wording was not abrogated. His saying: 'And consensus upon them'—you know what he mentioned in Sakh al-Anhur that consensus was not on the obligation of expiations, so the author of al-Multaqa counted expiatory fasting as wajib. His saying: 'So it is fard in the most apparent'—i.e., practical fard, because absolute consensus does not give definitive obligation. And it was said it is wajib because the verse 'And they should fulfill their vows' was specified for vows not of the same type as wajib, so it became non-definitive. In short, the two views are both preponderant. (Admonition: Necessary fasting has thirteen types: seven of them require consecutiveness: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for oath, expiation for dhihar, expiation for breaking fast in Ramadan, specific and non-specific vows if he undertook consecutiveness or intended it. However, if he breaks during them, he restarts and resumes. Ramadan and specific vow do not require restarting. Six do not require consecutiveness: qada' of Ramadan, fasting of expiation for shaving, fasting of penalty for hunting, fasting of absolute vow without mention of consecutiveness or intention, and fasting of oath by saying 'By Allah, I will fast a month.' This is the summary from the commentary of al-Sayyid.)
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage is from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, commenting on the description and division of fasting. The author begins by discussing the wisdom of fasting: it fosters empathy for the poor, as the fasting person experiences hunger, leading to compassion. Overeating at suhoor is discouraged as it defeats the purpose. Fasting is also an attribute of the angels, as they do not eat or drink. The author then addresses riya (show-off) in fasting, citing a hadith qudsi that Allah says 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it,' indicating that riya does not enter into fasting as it does in other acts. However, he clarifies that all obligatory acts are free from riya in terms of fulfilling the obligation; the special mention for fasting is for the degree of sincerity. The hadith covers both obligatory and voluntary fasting. The passage then moves to the classification of fasting into six types (detailed as eight): fard (including Ramadan and its qada, expiations, and nadhr), wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. The author explains that expiatory fasts are fard in practice but not in belief, so denying them does not make one a disbeliever. For nadhr (vowed fasts), there is a dispute: the most apparent view is that it is fard (practical), but some say it is wajib because the verse on vows is not definitive. The tanbih lists thirteen types of necessary fasting, detailing which require consecutive days and which do not.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically refraining, legally a specific act of worship abstaining from eating, drinking, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention.
فرض (fard): An action established by a definitive (qat'i) proof; denial leads to disbelief. In this passage, fard includes Ramadan, expiations (practical fard), and vowed fasts (in one view).
واجب (wajib): An action established by a non-definitive (zanni) proof; denial does not lead to disbelief. In this passage, some consider vowed fasts as wajib.
كفارة (kaffarah): A compensatory act (fasting, feeding, freeing a slave) required for specific violations of shari'ah, e.g., breaking an oath, dhihar, accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is fasting said to be free from riya in a special way compared to other acts of worship?
A) Because fasting is the most difficult act of worship
B) Because only Allah knows the true state of a fasting person, as stated in the hadith qudsi
C) Because riya is only possible in visible acts, and fasting is hidden
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly commanded no riya in fasting

Key Takeaway
Fasting classification in Hanafi fiqh is nuanced; the level of obligation depends on the type, with Ramadan as the clearest fard ayn, expiations as fard in practice, and vowed fasts as fard (practical) or wajib, all within a spectrum of evidentiary strength.

Fard Fasting in Hanafi Fiqh: Categories and Evidence
Critical refinement of fard fasting categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of fard fasting in the Hanafi school, identify whether each is fard 'ayn or fard 'amali, and cite the evidence (dalil) for each.

Core Idea
Fard fasting includes Ramadan, expiations, and vows – only Ramadan is fard 'ayn; the rest are fard 'amali due to evidence strength.

In this passage from his Hashiya, al-Tahtawi refines the base text's classification of obligatory fasts. He distinguishes between fasting that is fard 'ayn (individual obligation, definitive evidence) and that which is fard 'amali (practical obligation, consensus not fully definitive). The key is understanding how the Hanafi school grades obligation based on the certainty of transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of fard fasts
Read the passage and note the three kinds: Ramadan fast (performance and qada), expiatory fasts (kaffarat), and vowed fasts (nadhr).
Expiatory fasts include zihar, accidental killing, broken oath, hunting penalty, and harm compensation during ihram.
2
Determine the evidence (dalil) for each
For Ramadan, the evidence is qat'i (Quran, Sunnah, ijma). For expiations, the Quranic verses are qat'i in chain and text, but the ijma on their obligation is not fully definitive. For vows, the general command (wa yufu bi nadhrihim) is used, but some argue it is not qat'i.
Al-Tahtawi notes that expiations are fard 'amali because ijma is not absolute.
3
Understand fard 'ayn vs fard 'amali
Fard 'ayn is an individual obligation whose denial leads to disbelief (kufr). Fard 'amali is obligatory in practice but denial does not cause disbelief, because the evidence is not qat'i in one aspect.
This distinction is unique to Hanafis.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on vowed fasts
The text presents two views: the azhar (most apparent) is that the vowed fast is fard 'amali; another view says it is wajib. Al-Tahtawi considers both views balanced.
The mufta bihi is fard 'amali.

Source Text

لكونه أوقع في النفس (فرض) عين (وواجب ومسنون ومندوب ونفل ومكروه أما القسم الأول وهو (الفرض فهو صوم شهر رمضان أداء وقضاء (وصوم الكفارات) الظهار، والقتل واليمين وجزاء الصيد وفدية الأذى في الإحرام لثبوت هذه بالقاطع من الأدلة سنداً، ومتناً والإجماع عليها (و) من هذا القسم الصوم (المنذور) فهو فرض (في الأظهر) لقوله تعالى : معين، وهو صومه قضاء والواجب كذلك فالمعين كالنذر المعين وغير المعين كالنذر المطلق أفاده في الدر. قوله : (ذكرت) أي الأقسام مجملة أي لم يبين فيها الأفراد، ثم مفصلة ببيان أفرادها قوله : ( لكونه أوقع في النفس أي لكون التفصيل المفهوم من قوله مفصلة، وذلك لذكره بعد الاشتياق إلى البيان قوله : (وصوم (الكفارات لكنه فرض . عملاً لا اعتقاداً، ولذا لا يكفر جاحده در . قوله: (الظهار ) أي كفارة الظهار الخ وقوله : والقتل أي الخطأ، ومثله كفارة الإفطار، وإنما لم يذكرها لأنها مثلها، وأما صوم المتعة والقرآن فليس من صوم الكفارات، وإن كان فرضاً فسقط ما في السيد قوله : وفدية الأذى) كما إذا حلق، أو لبس بعذر فإنه يخير بين الذبح والإطعام والصيام فإذا اختار الصوم كان فرضاً. قوله: الثبوت هذه بالقاطع) عله لكونها فرضاً إلا أن الإجماع لم ينعقد على فرضية الكفارات فلذا كان عملياً فيها كما في سكب الأنهر، والقاطع هو القرآن فالظهار في المجادلة، والقتل في النساء، واليمين في المائدة وكذا جزاء الصيد وفدية الأذى في البقرة في قوله تعالى : فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو به أذى من رأسه) [البقرة: ٢] الآية. قوله : (سنداً) أي رجالاً، والمراد بقطعية السند أن رجاله ثقات وبقطعية المتن أي اللفظ أنه لم ينسخ بغيره، ولم يعارضه ما هو أقوى منه مما يدل عليه. قوله: (والإجماع عليها قد علمت ما ذكره في سكب الأنهر من أنّ الإجماع لم ينعقد على فرضية الكفارات حتى عد صاحب الملتقى صوم الكفارات من الواجب قوله : (فهو فرض في الأظهر) أي فرض عملي لأنّ مطلق الإجماع لا يفيد الفرض القطعي در، وقيل أنه واجب لأنه خص من آية وليوفوا نذورهم النذر بما ليس من جنسه واجب كعيادة المريض، فلم يبق قطعياً، وصار كخير الواحد، وبمثله يثبت الوجوب لا الفرض كذا في الشرح والحاصل أن القولين مرجحان .
Because it is more impactful in the soul: (Fard) 'ayn, (Wajib), (Sunnah), (Mandub), (Nafl), and (Makruh). As for the first category, which is Fard, it is the fast of Ramadan, performed on time and made up, and the fasts of expiations: zihar, killing, oath, penalty of hunting, and compensation for harm during ihram, because these are established by definitive evidence in chain and text, and by consensus. And from this category is the vowed fast, and it is Fard in the most apparent view due to His statement: [Quran] ... (commentary follows explaining the technical terms, the practical nature of fard for expiations, the lack of definitive consensus, and the difference of opinion on whether a vowed fast is fard or wajib, concluding that the two opinions are balanced).
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah is a critical refinement of the base text's classification of fasting types. The author first states the general categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. He then focuses on the first category, fard. Fard fasting includes: (1) the fast of Ramadan—both the month itself and its makeup (qada), (2) expiatory fasts (kaffarat) for zihar, accidental killing, broken oaths, hunting violations in ihram, and harm-related fidyah during ihram, (3) vowed fasts (nadhr). The proof for the obligation of these fasts is the Quran (verses from Surah al-Mujadilah, al-Nisa', al-Ma'idah, and al-Baqarah) and consensus (ijma). However, al-Tahtawi clarifies that for expiatory fasts, the ijma is not definitive (qat'i), so their fard status is practical ('amali) rather than creedal ('itikadi). Thus, denying these fasts does not make one a disbeliever. Regarding the vowed fast, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the stronger view (al-azhar) treats it as fard 'amali, while another opinion considers it wajib. Both views are considered valid (mutarajjihan). This passage highlights the precision of Hanafi legal theory in distinguishing levels of obligation based on the strength of evidence.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligation established by definitive (qat'i) evidence; here, fard 'amali means practically obligatory even if the proof is not absolutely certain in transmission or meaning.
كفارة (kaffarah): An expiatory fast prescribed for specific violations such as zihar, accidental killing, broken oath, hunting in ihram, and harm during ihram.
نذر (nadhr): A vow; when one vows to fast, it becomes obligatory. There is a dispute whether it is fard or wajib; the text adopts the view that it is fard 'amali.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what is the legal status of the fast of Ramadan?
A) Fard 'ayn
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah
D) Mandub

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fard fasting comprises Ramadan (fard 'ayn), expiations (fard 'amali), and vows (fard 'amali in the relied-upon view), with the level of obligation tied to the certainty of the evidence.

Which Obligatory Fasts Require Consecutive Days?
Enumeration and classification of necessary fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the thirteen necessary fasts into those that require consecutive days and those that do not, and state the consequence of breaking continuity for each category.

Core Idea
Not all obligatory fasts require consecutive days; only seven do, and among those, the effect of interruption differs.

In the Hanafi school, certain fasts must be observed on consecutive days; breaking the sequence may force a restart. This note from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya provides a comprehensive list of thirteen necessary fasts, specifying which require consecutive days and which do not. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly fulfilling one's obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
First, note that the author divides necessary fasts into two groups: those requiring consecutive days (tatabu') and those not. There are seven in the first group and six in the second.
The total is thirteen types.
2
Examine the seven that require consecutiveness
List them: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking fast in Ramadan, specified vow if consecutive is intended, and unspecified vow if consecutive is intended.
These seven are further subdivided based on the effect of breaking continuity.
3
Distinguish the restart rule within the seven
For Ramadan and specified vow, if consecutiveness is broken, you do not need to restart. For the other five (kill, zihar, break fast, oath, absolute vow with intent), breaking consecutiveness requires restarting from the beginning.
This distinction is important for practical application.
4
Identify the six that do not require consecutiveness
These are: makeup of Ramadan, expiation for shaving, expiation for hunting, absolute vow without mention/intent of consecutiveness, and vow made as an oath (e.g., 'By Allah I will fast a month').
For these, fasting may be done non-consecutively.

Attention: Necessary fasts are thirteen types. Seven of them require consecutive days (tatabu'): Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking a fast in Ramadan, and both specified and unspecified vows if one commits to consecutiveness or intends it. However, for fasts of expiation for killing, zihar, breaking a fast, oath, and absolute vow, if one mentions consecutiveness or intends it and then breaks the fast in the middle, he must start over (istaqbala wa ista'nafa). For Ramadan and specified vow, restart is not required if continuity is broken. Six types do not require consecutiveness: makeup of Ramadan, fast of expiation for shaving (halq), fast of penalty for hunting, absolute vow without mentioning or intending consecutiveness, and a vow of an oath (e.g., 'By Allah I will fast a month'). This is the summary from Sharh al-Sayyid. He said: 'It is the makeup of tamattu''.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This note from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya provides a detailed enumeration of necessary fasts and their requirement for consecutive days (tatabu'). It is a critical refinement because the base text's classification is expanded. The author lists seven fasts that must be observed consecutively: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking a Ramadan fast, and vowed fasts (specified or unspecified) if one either commits to consecutiveness or intends it. However, a distinction is made: for Ramadan and specified vows, if one breaks the continuity (e.g., by an invalidating act), he does not need to start the entire fast over; he can continue. For the other five (kill, zihar, break fast, oath, absolute vow with intent of consecutiveness), if consecutiveness is broken, the entire series must be restarted. Six fasts do not require consecutiveness: makeup of Ramadan, expiation for shaving (halq), penalty for hunting, absolute vow without intention of consecutiveness, and a vow made as an oath (e.g., swearing to fast a month). The note ends abruptly with a comment about 'qada al-mut'ah' (makeup of tamattu'), likely referring to the expiation for tamattu' in Hajj.

Key Terms
تتابع (tatabu'): The requirement to fast consecutive days without interruption; for some fasts, breaking the sequence necessitates restarting from the beginning.
الصوم اللازم (al-sawm al-lazim): Necessary fasts that are obligatory upon a person, distinguished by specific conditions of continuity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The requirement of consecutive days (tatabu') applies only to certain obligatory fasts, and among those, the effect of breaking continuity varies.

Wajib Fasting: Qada of Nafl and Vowed I'tikaf
Definition of wajib fasting in Hanafi classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of wajib fasting in the Hanafi school and explain why each is considered wajib.

Core Idea
Starting a nafl fast or vowing i'tikaf makes fasting wajib — obligation arises from intention.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah defines the second category of fasting: wajib. While fard fasting is proven by definitive evidence (e.g., Ramadan), wajib fasting arises from secondary causes. The text identifies two scenarios: making up a spoiled voluntary fast, and fasting for a vowed i'tikaf. Understanding this classification is key to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that the passage defines 'wajib' fasting. Distinguish wajib from fard: wajib is established by probabilistic evidence or secondary causes.
Wajib is a lower level of obligation than fard in Hanafi usul.
2
Note the first type: qada of spoiled nafl
Once a nafl fast is commenced, it becomes wajib to complete. If broken, making it up (qada) becomes wajib.
This applies to all voluntary acts: commencement creates obligation.
3
Note the second type: fast of vowed i'tikaf
If one vows to perform i'tikaf, fasting is a condition for its validity in the Hanafi school. Thus the fast itself becomes wajib due to the vow.
Vows (nudhur) create obligations in Islamic law.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle
Both types illustrate that obligation can arise from human acts of commencement (shuru') or speech (nadh). This is distinct from the primary obligation of Ramadan.
This principle is rooted in hadith: 'Whoever starts a voluntary act must complete it.'
5
Recognize practical consequences
Knowing that these are wajib, not just nafl, affects whether one must make up a broken fast and whether i'tikaf is valid without fasting.
The mufta bihi is that qada is required for broken nafl fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما ) القسم الثاني وهو الواجب) فهو قضاء ما أفسده من) صوم (نفل) لوجوبه بالشروع وصوم الاعتكاف المنذور
As for the second category, which is Wajib: it is the makeup of what one has spoiled of a nafl fast, due to its being obligatory upon commencement, and the fast of a vowed i'tikaf.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This brief statement from the base matn of Maraqi al-Falah, critically annotated by al-Tahtawi, defines the second category of fasting: wajib. In Hanafi fiqh, a wajib act is an obligation established by probabilistic (zanni) evidence, or an obligation that arises from a secondary cause. Here, two types are mentioned: (1) The makeup (qada) of a nafl (voluntary) fast that one has spoiled (afsadahu). Once a person begins a nafl fast, it becomes wajib to complete it; if it is invalidated, making it up becomes wajib. This is because the act of commencement created a new obligation. (2) The fast of a vowed i'tikaf (al-i'tikaf al-mandhur). If one vows to perform i'tikaf, fasting becomes a necessary condition for its validity according to the Hanafi school, and thus the fast itself becomes wajib. The commentary in the Hashiya elaborates elsewhere, but the matn itself is concise.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): An obligatory act that is proven by probabilistic evidence or becomes obligatory due to a secondary cause, such as starting a nafl act or making a vow.
قضاء (qada): The makeup of a fast that was invalidated after having been commenced, such as a broken nafl fast.
الاعتكاف المنذور (al-i'tikaf al-mandhur): A vowed seclusion in the mosque; according to Hanafis, fasting is a condition for its validity, making the fast wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because once commenced, the fast becomes wajib to complete
C) Because the Prophet (SAW) commanded it
D) Because it is a vow

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, wajib fasting includes the makeup of a spoiled nafl fast and the fast of a vowed i'tikaf — both obligations arise from the act of commencing or vowing.

Sunnah Fasting on Ashura and the Ninth of Muharram
Explanation of sunnah fasting on Ashura and the 9th with evidence and rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on fasting Ashura and the 9th of Muharram, and explain why adding a day before or after is recommended.

Core Idea
Fasting Ashura is sunnah muakkadah; to avoid resembling Jews, also fast the 9th or 11th.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the sunnah fasting on Ashura (10th Muharram) and the recommendation to also fast the 9th (or 11th) based on the Prophet's practice and statement. It also discusses the expiation of minor sins and the distinction from Jewish practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of fasting
Recognize that Ashura fasting is classified as sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah).
2
State the ruling on Ashura
Fasting on the 10th of Muharram is sunnah and expiates minor sins of the past year.
Expiation is for minor sins only.
3
Note the recommendation to fast the 9th
The Prophet ﷺ fasted the 10th and intended to fast the 9th, so it is sunnah to fast both or add the 11th.
Hadith: 'If I live until next year, I will fast the ninth.'
4
Understand the rationale
Adding a day before or after removes the dislike (karahah) of fasting only on Ashura, which resembles Jewish practice.
The Hanafi school avoids tashabbuh (resemblance) with non-Muslims.
5
Compare with Arafah fast
The fast of Arafah expiates two years (past and future) and is Muhammadan legislation, unlike Ashura which is Mosaic.
This shows the superiority of Arafah in terms of reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما ) القسم الثالث ، وهو (المسنون فهو صوم عاشوراء) فإنه يكفر السنة الماضية (مع) صوم (التاسع) لصومه العاشر، وقال لئن : بقيت إلى قابل لأصومن التاسع قوله : ( فإنه يكفر السنة الماضية) والمراد الصغائر وأما صوم يوم عرفة فيكفر ذنوب سنتين الماضية والآتية لأنه شرع محمدي بخلاف الأول فإنه شرع موسوي وعد صاحب الدر صوم عرفة من المندوب. قوله : (مع صوم التاسع) أي أو الحادي عشر لما يأتي للمصنف فتنتفي الكراهة بضم يوم قبله، أو بعده. قوله : (لئن بقيت إلى قابل) أي إلى عام قابل ولم يبق إليه .
As for the third category, which is Sunnah: it is the fast of Ashura, for it expiates the past year, with the fast of the ninth, because he (the Prophet) fasted the tenth and said: 'If I live until next year, I will surely fast the ninth.' Commentary: He said: 'It expiates the past year' – meaning minor sins. The fast of Arafah expiates sins of two years, past and future, because it is Muhammadan legislation, unlike the former which is Mosaic. And the author of al-Durr counted the fast of Arafah as mandub. He said: 'with the fast of the ninth' – meaning or the eleventh, as the author will mention, so the dislike (karahah) is removed by adding a day before or after. He said: 'If I live until next year' – meaning until the coming year, but he did not live to do so.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah discusses the third category: sunnah fasting. The base text states that fasting on Ashura (the 10th of Muharram) is sunnah and expiates the minor sins of the past year. It also recommends fasting on the 9th (tasi') because the Prophet ﷺ fasted the 10th and intended to fast the 9th if he lived. The commentary clarifies that expiation is for minor sins only, and compares with the fast of Arafah, which expiates two years. The commentator notes that fasting the 9th is not exclusive; one may also fast the 11th to distinguish from Jews. The karahah (dislike) of fasting only on Ashura is removed by adding a day before or after. The hadith about the Prophet's statement is quoted as evidence. This illustrates the Hanafi tendency to follow prophetic practice and to avoid resemblance to non-Muslims (tashabbuh).

Key Terms
مسنون (masnun): A recommended act that the Prophet ﷺ performed consistently; here it is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah).
عاشوراء ('Ashura): The 10th day of Muharram; fasting on it is sunnah and expiates minor sins of the previous year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Mandub Fasts: White Days, Monday/Thursday, and Six of Shawwal
Description of mandub fasts and their proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of mandub fasts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding fasting six days of Shawwal.

Core Idea
Six of Shawwal: Imam Abu Hanifa deemed it makruh; later scholars made it mandub.

The Hanafi school classifies acts beyond obligations into various levels of recommendation. This lesson examines the fourth category: mandub (recommended) fasts. The text lists three specific types: three days per month (preferably the 'white days'), Monday and Thursday, and six days of Shawwal. A notable internal disagreement exists regarding the six days of Shawwal—Imam Abu Hanifa considered them makruh, but later Hanafi scholars accepted them as mandub. Understanding this illustrates how later scholarly consensus can refine earlier positions within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three mandub fasts
The text explicitly names three recommended fasts: three days every month (preferably the 13th, 14th, 15th), Monday and Thursday, and six days of Shawwal. For each, note the hukm level: mandub.
Mandub is a category between sunnah and mubah.
2
Locate the proof for each
For the white days, the text cites a hadith from Abu Dawud. For Monday/Thursday, a hadith about presentation of deeds. For six of Shawwal, the famous hadith: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan and follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is like fasting the whole year.'
All proofs are ahad hadith, indicating recommendation, not obligation.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on six of Shawwal
Imam Abu Hanifa considered fasting six days of Shawwal makruh (tanzihi). Later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) saw no harm and considered it mandub. The text mentions the source 'al-Bahr' and 'aamat al-muta'akhkhirin' for the permissive view.
This is a refinement (critical refinement) of an earlier position.
4
Understand the tarjih mechanism
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the later view: fasting six days of Shawwal is mandub and not makruh. This illustrates that fatwa can shift based on later scholarly consensus and practical considerations.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

. قوله: (كان كصيام الدهر) لأن جملة ما صامه برمضان ستة وثلاثون يوماً كل يوم بعشر فهي ثلثمائة وستون يوماً، وهي عدد أيام السنة، والمراد أنه يحصل له ثواب عظيم، وإن اختلفت الكيفية، فإنه لا شك أن هي.
As for the fourth category, which is Mandub: it is the fast of three days every month, so that it becomes like fasting the whole month, because 'whoever brings a good deed shall have ten times its like.' It is recommended that they be the white days: the 13th, 14th, and 15th. They are so named because of the completeness of the moonlight and intense whiteness. As narrated in Abu Dawud: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ used to command us to fast the white days: the 13th, 14th, and 15th.' He said: 'It is like fasting the entire year.' And from this category is the fast of Monday and Thursday, due to his saying: 'Deeds are presented on Monday and Thursday, and I love that my deeds be presented while I am fasting.' And from it is the fast of six days of Shawwal, due to his saying: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan and follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is like fasting the whole year.' Then it is said: The best is to make up what one spoiled... [commentary follows explaining the virtues, the linguistic meaning of 'white days', the dislike of fasting six of Shawwal according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but later scholars did not see any harm.]
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This final passage from the Hashiya covers the fourth category: mandub (recommended) fasts. The base matn lists three types: (1) Three days every month – preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th) because the Prophet ﷺ commanded them, and they are equivalent to fasting the whole year due to the ten-fold reward. (2) Monday and Thursday – because deeds are presented on those days. (3) Six days of Shawwal – following Ramadan makes it like fasting the entire year. The commentary provides several points: the white days are named for the moon's brightness; the term 'white' refers to the nights; there is a difference of opinion on six of Shawwal – Imam Abu Hanifa considered them makruh (reprehensible) whether spread out or consecutive, but later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) saw no problem. The Hashiya also repeats some earlier commentary about Ashura (likely a copying error). The passage ends abruptly with incomplete sentence. Overall, it illustrates the Hanafi classification of mandub acts and the internal debate on certain practices, showing how later scholarly consensus can override earlier individual positions.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act; it is meritorious but not as strongly encouraged as sunnah mu'akkadah.
الأيام البيض (al-ayyam al-bid): The white days: 13th, 14th, 15th of each lunar month; recommended for fasting due to the brightness of the moon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Classification of Fasting Types: Wajib to Makruh
Systematic categorization of fasting with proofs and intra-school ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify fasting into its six Hanafi categories (wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi), cite the evidence for each, and state the relied-upon ruling for the Shawwal six-day fast.

Core Idea
Fasting falls on a spectrum from obligatory to prohibited; knowing the category determines the ruling and reward.

In this passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi, we encounter a systematic Hanafi classification of fasting types. Unlike a simple list, the text distinguishes between obligations incurred by starting a voluntary fast, highly recommended fasts (like Ashura), commendable ones (three days, Monday/Thursday, six days of Shawwal), general voluntary fasts, and disliked fasts. We will also see an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the six days of Shawwal and how later scholars resolved it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
Scan the passage and list the six categories in order: wajib (obligatory), masnun (recommended), mandub (commendable), nafl (supererogatory), makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked), makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The first category (fard) is omitted as it was covered earlier.
2
Understand the basis of each category
For each category, note the key ruling and the evidence cited (Quran or hadith). Wajib: making up a broken voluntary fast (obligation by commencement). Masnun: Ashura (expiates minor sins of past year). Mandub: three days per month (like whole year), Monday/Thursday (deeds presented), six days of Shawwal (like fasting all year). Nafl: any voluntary fast not specified as disliked. Makruh: divided into tanzihi (Ashura alone, singling out Friday/Saturday) and tahrimi (Eids, Tashriq).
The proofs are hadith-based; commands indicate recommendation not obligation.
3
Analyze the masnun fast of Ashura
Note that Ashura expiates minor sins of the past year and should be paired with the 9th or 11th to avoid dislike. This shows the Hanafi concern for avoiding resemblance to other communities.
The text contrasts Ashura (Mosaic) with Arafah (Muhammadan) which expiates two years.
4
Examine the mandub category and its sub-types
Identify the three sub-types of mandub: three days per month (preferably the white days), Monday/Thursday, and six days of Shawwal. Note the hadith for each. For Shawwal, the text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute.
The white days are 13th, 14th, 15th; hadith from Abu Dawud.
5
Study the Shawwal dispute and its resolution
Imam Abu Hanifa considered fasting six days of Shawwal makruh (whether consecutive or separate) due to resemblance to adding to the obligatory. Later scholars (mutakakhkhirun) saw no problem, citing the explicit hadith. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that it is commendable and not disliked, with separation being better but consecutive permissible.
The tarjih authority is al-Bahr and al-Tanwir, relying on later fatwa.

Source Text

كتاب الصوم

الجمعة
ثلاثة
ثواب الصائم بالفعل أكثر لأنّ صوم كل يوم بعشرة فهي تزيد على ما ذكر بأضعاف كثيرة. قوله: الظاهر قوله فاتبع أي والوصل فيه تحقيق تمام المتابعة. قوله: (وقيل تفريقها) قال في التنوير وشرحه وندب تفريق صوم الست من شوال ولا يكره التتابع على المختار خلافاً للثاني حاوي. قوله : ( في التشبيه الأولى حذفه ويقول في الزيادة ويكون متعلقاً بالمخالفة . قوله : (وأحبه) أي أكثره ثواباً قوله : (كان) ينام الخ) في نسخة بواو وفي نسخ بحذفها، أو هو الذي في السيد والشرح قوله وينام سدسه ليقوم لصلاة الفجر بنشاط ، ويقوم بوظائف الأذكار بعده. قوله : ( وكان يفطر يوماً ويصوم يوماً) لئلا تعتاد النفس على الصيام فيصير طبعاً. قوله : (ولا تخصيصه أي ولا طلب صومه مخصصاً بوقت قوله: (ومنه صوم أيام التشريق) هي النحر قوله : ( وكره إفراد يوم الجمعة إلا أن يضم إليه يوماً قبله، أو بعده كما في الحديث، واعلم أنه ثبت بالسنة طلب صومه والنهي عنه، والأخير منهما النهي كما وضحة شرح الجامع الصغير للسيوطي، وذلك لأن فيه وظائف فلعله إذا صامه ضعف عن فعلها، وعد في الدر صومه من المندوب والمعتمد ما هنا قوله : (لا) تخصوا ليلة الجمعة) النهي للتنزيه والمعنى النهي عن الاستعداد لها بخصوصها، أما إذا كان اتفاقياً، فلا التعمد لا ينتفي الثواب . قوله: (إلا) أن يكون في صوم أي مع صوم قبله، أو بعده. قوله: (وكره إفراد يوم السبت) للتشبه باليهود بحر. قوله : (إلا فيما افترض عليكم مثله ما إذا ضم إليه غيره. قوله:
بعد
يوم
Book of Fasting. And to fulfill their vows. As for the second category, which is obligatory, it is making up what one has spoiled of a voluntary fast, due to its obligation by commencing, and the fast of a vowed retreat. As for the third category, which is recommended (masnun), it is the fast of Ashura, for it expiates the past year, with the fast of the ninth, because he fasted the tenth, and he said: If I live until next year, I will fast the ninth. As for the fourth category, which is commendable (mandub), it is fasting three days of every month, to be like fasting all of it: 'Whoever brings a good deed shall have ten times its like.' It is commendable that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth; they were so named due to the completeness of the crescent's light and the intensity of whiteness in them, because of what is in Abu Dawud: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to command us to fast the white days: the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth. He said: And he said: It is like fasting all time—i.e., like fasting all life. And of this category is fasting Monday and Thursday, due to his saying: Deeds are presented on Monday and Thursday, so I love that my deeds be presented while I am fasting. And of it is fasting six days of Shawwal, due to his saying: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan and then follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is like fasting all time.' Then it is said: The best is to fast what one has spoiled, and likewise completing it after beginning it, as indicated by al-Sayyid. His saying: 'For it expiates the past year' means minor sins; as for the fast of Arafah, it expiates the sins of two years, past and future, because it is Muhammadan legislation, unlike the former which is Mosaic. The author of al-Durr counted the fast of Arafah as commendable. His saying: 'With the fast of the ninth' means or the eleventh, as the author will mention later; the dislike is removed by joining a day before or after. His saying: 'If I live until next year' means until the coming year, but he did not live until it. His saying: 'Whoever brings' He brought it as proof for his saying: 'like fasting all of it' as if he said due to the verse 'Whoever brings'. His saying: 'It is commendable that they be the white days' indicates that three days of the month, whichever they are, are commendable, and that they specifically be these days is another commendable; so whoever fasts other than them has performed one of the commendable acts. His saying: 'By that' means by the white. His saying: 'The completeness of the crescent's light' so the meaning is the whiteness of their night; so it is better to say 'days of the white' meaning days of the white nights. His saying: 'That we fast the white' means the white days; his saying: 'thirteen' with masculine for the single and feminine for ten in all is a substitute for 'white', and its referent is the nights. His saying: 'He said' means the narrator; his saying: 'And he said' means the Prophet. His saying: 'like fasting all time' because every day is ten, so as if he fasted the whole month, and whoever accustoms himself to them, it is as if he fasted all time. His saying: 'Fasting Monday and Thursday' even for a needy person who is not weakened by fasting, as said by al-Sayyid. His saying: 'Deeds are presented' means the recording angels present them to one another; whatever is good or bad is recorded, and whatever is permissible is removed. His saying: 'And of it is fasting six days of Shawwal' al-Bahr said: Fasting six of Shawwal is disliked (makruh) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, whether separately or consecutively, but most later scholars saw no problem with it. His saying: 'It is like fasting all time' because the total of what he fasts in Ramadan is thirty-six days, each day ten, so that is three hundred sixty days, which is the number of days of the year; the meaning is that he attains immense reward, though the modality differs, for there is no doubt that... the reward of the one who fasts in reality is greater because each day of fasting is ten, so it exceeds what was mentioned by many multiples. His saying: 'The apparent meaning of his saying 'so follow it' means that joining achieves complete following. His saying: 'And it is said: separating them' said in al-Tanwir and its commentary: It is commendable to separate the six days of Shawwal, and consecutive fasting is not disliked according to the chosen view, contrary to the second opinion. His saying: 'In the resemblance' it is better to delete it and say 'in the increase' and it relates to opposition. His saying: 'And most beloved to Him' means most rewarding. His saying: 'He used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth' to rise for Fajr prayer actively and perform the functions of dhikr after it. His saying: 'And he used to break fast one day and fast one day' so that the soul does not become accustomed to fasting and become habitual. His saying: 'And nor its specification to a time' means nor the seeking of its fasting specifically to a time. His saying: 'And of it is fasting the days of al-Tashriq' they are the days of sacrifice. His saying: 'And it is disliked to single out Friday' unless one joins to it a day before or after, as in the hadith. Know that seeking its fast and prohibition are both established in the Sunnah, and the latter of the two is the prohibition, as clarified in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Suyuti. That is because there are functions on that day, and perhaps if one fasts it, he becomes weak to perform them. It is counted in al-Durr as commendable, but the relied-upon is what is here. His saying: 'Do not single out Friday night' the prohibition is for tanzih (dislike), meaning the prohibition is against intending it specifically; but if it is coincidental, then the reward is not negated by intent. His saying: 'Except that it be in a fast' meaning with a fast before or after. His saying: 'And it is disliked to single out Saturday' due to resemblance to the Jews, as in al-Bahr. His saying: 'Except for what is obligatory upon you' similarly when one joins another day to it. His saying: 'after' day.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi classifies fasting into five main categories following the Hanafi school. The first category (fard) is omitted as it was covered earlier. The second category is wajib (obligatory), which includes making up a broken voluntary fast (due to the obligation incurred by starting it) and fasting for a vowed retreat. The third is masnun (recommended), exemplified by the fast of Ashura (10th Muharram) which expiates minor sins of the past year; it is best paired with the 9th or 11th to avoid dislike. The fourth is mandub (commendable), which includes fasting three days each month—preferably the white days (13th, 14th, 15th) as per hadith—and also fasting Mondays, Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal. The text notes a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the six days of Shawwal: Imam Abu Hanifa considered it makruh (disliked) whether consecutive or separate, but later scholars (mutakakhkhirun) found no issue, and the chosen view is that it is commendable. The fifth is nafl (supererogatory), any fast not specified by the Lawgiver as disliked. The sixth is makruh (disliked), divided into tanzihi (lightly disliked) like fasting Ashura alone without the 9th/11th, and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) like fasting the two Eid days and days of Tashriq, due to explicit prohibition. The text also discusses the prohibition of singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting, requiring a day before or after to avoid resemblance to Jews or neglect of Friday duties. The author relies on the hadith and later Hanafi authorities for the final rulings.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): The act of fasting, abstaining from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory fasting, incurred by commencement of a voluntary fast (must be completed) or by vow.
مسنون (masnun): Recommended fasting that the Prophet consistently performed, like Ashura, with expiation of sins.
مندوب (mandub): Commendable fasting, encouraged but not consistently performed by the Prophet, e.g., three days per month, Monday/Thursday, six days of Shawwal.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory fasting beyond the above categories, without specific prohibition or time specification.
مكروه تنزيهاً (makruh tanzihan): Lightly disliked fasting, such as fasting Ashura alone without an additional day, or singling out Friday/Saturday.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): Prohibitively disliked fasting, approaching haram, such as fasting on the two Eids and days of Tashriq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (mutakakhkhirun) as presented in this passage, what is the ruling on fasting six days of Shawwal consecutively?
A) Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and must be avoided
B) Makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) but permissible
C) Commendable (mandub) and not disliked, whether consecutive or separate
D) Obligatory (wajib) due to the hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of fasting provides a precise framework; the later view on Shawwal fasting is the operative one for fatwa.
