Ruling for One Who Alone Sees the Crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal
Obligations and prohibitions for a solitary crescent sighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an individual who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the imam's conduct.

Core Idea
A solitary sighting obligates fasting for Ramadan but forbids breaking fast for Shawwal; the imam's certainty may justify public action.

This passage from classical Hanafi texts addresses a scenario where only one person witnesses the crescent. While the judge may reject the testimony for public announcement, the individual's personal knowledge creates binding obligations. The ruling draws on Quranic and hadith proofs and includes a key difference among Hanafi authorities regarding the imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: solitary sighting of the crescent. For Ramadan, the hukm is that fasting becomes wajib; for Shawwal, breaking fast is prohibited (haram) for the individual alone.
The ruling applies even if the judge rejects the testimony.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: for Ramadan, Quran 2:185 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' For Shawwal, the hadith 'Your fast is the day you fast and your breaking fast is the day you break fast' is used to prohibit breaking fast when the community has not yet broken.
The hadith indicates that the community's practice is binding.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe the disagreement between authorities: al-Jawhara holds that the imam who alone sees the crescent should not lead Eid prayer or break fast publicly; al-Hujjah (al-Quduri) states that if certain, the imam may go out, pray Eid, and break fast.
This difference concerns only the imam, not the common individual.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator al-Tahtawi presents both views but leans toward the permissibility for the imam to act on certainty, while noting that precaution (ihtiyat) is also recommended. The relied-upon position is from al-Hujjah.
The mufta bihi is that the imam may act publicly if certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن رأى هلال رمضان وحده أو هلال الفطر وحده ورد قوله أي رده القاضي الزمه الصيام لقوله تعالى: فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه وقد رآه ظاهراً لقوله: صومكم يوم تصومون وفطركم يوم تفطرون والناس لم يفطروا فواجب أن لا يفطر لا فرق بين كون السماء بعلة فلم يقبل لنفسه أو ردت بصحوها لانفراده وفيه إشارة إلى لزوم صيامه وإن لم يشهد عند القاضي ولا فرق بين كونه من عرض الناس أو الإمام فلا يأمر الناس بالصوم ولا بالفطر إذا رآه وحده ويصوم هو ولا يجوز له الفطر بتيقنه هلال شوال برؤيته منفرداً لما روبنا كذا في الفتح والتتارخانية عن المحيط والخلاصة وفي الجوهرة خلافه قال الإمام يأمرهم بالصوم برؤيته وحده ولا العيد ولا يفطر لا سراً ولا جهراً انتهى فأخذ بالاحتياط في المحلين وفي الحجة قال صاحب الكتاب: إذا استيقن بالهلال يخرج ويصلي العيد ويفطر لأنه ثابت بالشرع.
And whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan alone or the crescent of breaking the fast (Shawwal) alone, and his testimony is rejected, meaning the judge rejects it, fasting is incumbent upon him due to His saying: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it,' and he has seen it apparently, and due to His saying: 'Your fast is the day you fast and your breaking fast is the day you break fast,' and the people did not break fast, so it is obligatory that he not break fast. There is no difference between the sky being cloudy so he did not accept it for himself or it was rejected due to its clearness because of his isolation. And in it is an indication of the necessity of his fasting even if he did not testify before the judge. And there is no difference between him being from the common people or the imam; so he does not order the people to fast nor to break fast when he sees it alone, and he fasts himself. And it is not permissible for him to break fast by his certainty of the crescent of Shawwal by his solitary vision due to what we have narrated. Thus in al-Fath and al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Muhit and al-Khulasa, and in al-Jawhara there is a difference: the imam orders them to fast by his alone vision, and does not lead them in Eid nor does he break fast, neither secretly nor openly. End. So he adopted precaution in both matters. And in al-Hujjah, the author of the book said: When he is certain of the crescent, he goes out, prays Eid, and breaks fast because it is established by the Shariah.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for an individual who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal and whose testimony is rejected by the judge. For Ramadan, such a person must fast because the Quran says 'let him fast it' (2:185), and he has seen it even if others did not. He cannot break fast for Shawwal based on his alone sighting because the hadith 'your fast is the day you fast' indicates that the fast of the community is binding. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the author of al-Jawhara holds that if the imam alone sees the crescent of Ramadan, he orders the people to fast but does not lead Eid prayer and does not break fast even privately. The text from al-Hujjah (likely al-Quduri) states that if the imam is certain, he may go out, pray Eid, and break fast because it is established by the Shariah. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) notes that the precautionary approach is taken in both matters, but the relied-upon view supports the permissibility for the imam to act publicly if certain.

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): Rejection of testimony by the judge, meaning the witness's sighting is not accepted as sufficient for the public.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): Precautionary approach taken to avoid error; here, in the case of solitary sighting, the imam may choose to act with caution.
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty; here, the imam's personal certainty about the crescent sighting.

Sawm — Fasting

Ruling on Seeing the Crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal Alone
Commentary on ruling for one who sees hilal alone and testimony is rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone, and explain the proof and the precautionary approach adopted.

Core Idea
Your fast is with the community, but the Quranic command binds you to fast if you witness the month alone.

This commentary from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a delicate situation: what if you are absolutely certain you saw the crescent — either for Ramadan or Shawwal — but the judge rejects your testimony because you are alone? The passage navigates between personal certainty and communal practice, relying on a key hadith: 'Your fast is the day you fast, and your breaking of fast is the day you break fast.' Yet for Ramadan, the Quranic verse 2:185 overrides the general rule, making the individual bound to fast. For Shawwal, he must not break fast until the community does. This lesson unpacks the proof, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and the precautionary (ihtiyat) resolution that leads to the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Understand that the ruling has two distinct parts: (1) seeing the crescent of Ramadan alone, and (2) seeing the crescent of Shawwal alone. For each, determine the basic ruling (fasting vs. breaking fast).
The same person is involved: one whose testimony is rejected by the judge.
2
Locate the primary proof for each
For Ramadan, the proof is Quran 2:185: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' For Shawwal, the proof is the hadith: 'Your fast is the day you fast and your breaking of fast is the day you break fast.' The commentary addresses a potential objection about the hadith overriding the personal sighting.
The hadith teaches alignment with the community, but the Quranic verse creates an exception for the individual.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a difference among Hanafi authorities. The cautious view (from al-Muhit, al-Khulasa, and adopted by al-Tahtawi) says the imam cannot act on his private sighting, neither for Ramadan nor Shawwal. The opposing view (from al-Jawhara and al-Quduri) says if the imam is certain, he may command others and even break fast and lead Eid prayer.
The commentator explicitly takes the cautious position, saying 'precaution was taken in both matters.'
4
Understand the effect of shubha on kaffarah
If the person breaks fast on Shawwal due to his certainty, he must make up the day but no expiation (kaffarah) is required. This is because of the legal doubt (shubha) — he believed it was Eid. The passage mentions a minority view that kaffarah is due.
The hadith 'Your fast is the day you fast' creates a shubha that blocks the hadd (penalty).

Book of Fasting. Sha'ban with worship as it is recommendable in every month. And whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan alone or the crescent of Shawwal alone and his statement is rejected (i.e., the judge rejects it), fasting is binding upon him because of the saying of the Exalted: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And he saw it apparently because of his saying: 'Your fast is the day you fast and your breaking of fast is the day you break fast.' And the people did not break fast, so it is obligatory that he not break fast. There is no difference between the sky having a veil (clouds) such that he was not accepted for himself, or it being clear and he was rejected due to his being alone. And in it is an indication of the necessity of his fast even if he did not testify before the judge. And there is no difference between him being from the common people or the imam: he does not command the people to fast nor to break fast when he sees it alone, and he fasts. And it is not permissible for him to break fast with certainty of the crescent of Shawwal by his seeing it alone, because of what we narrated. Thus in al-Fath and al-TatarKhaniyya from al-Muhit and al-Khulasa. And in al-Jawhara there is a contrary view: he (the imam) commands them to fast by his seeing it alone, and he does not (lead) the Eid prayer, and he does not break fast secretly or openly. End. So precaution was taken in both matters. And in al-Hujja, the author of the book said: 'If he is certain of the crescent, he goes out, prays the Eid prayer, and breaks fast because it is established by Shari'ah.' Intention of obligation (i.e., regarding when he precedes the fast of Ramadan intending it to be from it). His saying: 'And the hadith of the white days (ayyam al-beed)' meaning the hadith indicating the request to fast the white days. His saying: 'closure of Sha'ban' is the predicate of 'anna'. His saying: 'that' meaning the closure with the worship of fasting. His saying: 'rejected'. His saying: 'so if he did not reject, he fasts it all the more'. His saying: 'fasting is binding on him'. Similarly it is binding on his friend if he informs him of his sighting and he believes him, he should not break fast. And if he breaks fast, there is no expiation upon him. Al-Bahr. His saying: 'And because of his saying, your fast etc.' is the proof for the second issue. His saying: 'the day you break fast' with fatha on the ta' as evidence for iftar. If it were with dammah, he would have said 'and your iftar'. And in al-Qamus: 'The fasting person breaks his fast by eating and drinking like afṭara, and faṭṭartahu with tashdid and khaffafa, and afṭartuhu.' End. And it was raised that the hadith indicates that the fast is the day the people fast, and whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan alone and his statement is rejected, his fast is obligatory even though the people did not fast. And it was answered that the fast is established by a specific proof, which is the preceding verse. His saying: 'and in it is an indication' etc. Its aspect is that if fasting is binding on him after his statement is rejected, it binds him if he did not testify and did not reject all the more. And the fast intended here is its reality, not abstention, according to the relied-upon view in the case of seeing the crescent of Shawwal. And is it obligatory or recommended? Two sayings, and the first is relied-upon. And by obligation is meant farḍiyyah, as said by the author of Tuhfat al-Akhyar. His saying: 'from the common people' with dammah, meaning their general public as in al-Qamus. His saying: 'when he sees it' meaning the crescent of fasting or the crescent of breaking fast according to distribution. His saying: 'and it is not permissible for him to break fast' makes the speech of the author connected to what came before from the issue of the imam, so he takes the text out of generality. His saying: 'and in al-Jawhara' and similarly in al-Hidayah from al-Siraj. His saying: 'he said' meaning the author of al-Jawhara. His saying: 'by his sighting' meaning by seeing the crescent of Ramadan. His saying: 'and he does not lead them in Eid prayer' meaning when he sees the crescent of Shawwal as clarified in al-Siraj. And similarly it is said afterward. His saying: 'so he took' meaning he took this distinction. His saying: 'in the two matters' are seeing the crescent of Ramadan for fasting, and seeing the crescent of breaking fast for fasting also, due to the possibility of error in sighting. His saying: 'the author of the book said' probably he is al-Quduri. His saying: 'if he is certain' meaning the imam. His saying: 'because it is established by Shari'ah' meaning by the sighting of the imam... And if he breaks fast, one who sees the crescent alone in both times (Ramadan and Shawwal) makes it up because of what we recited, and there is no expiation upon him nor upon the friend of the seer if he testified before him about the crescent of Shawwal and he believed him and broke fast, because it is the day of Eid in his view, so it becomes a doubt. And his testimony is rejected in Ramadan, he is legally deemed a liar. And thereby there is no expiation upon him, even if his breaking fast was before the judge rejected it in the correct view, due to the existence of the doubt, which is his saying: 'The fast is the day you fast.' And it was said: expiation is obligatory in both cases, due to the apparent among people in the breaking of fast and the reality that he has in Ramadan.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Hashiya al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, addressing the ruling for a person who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone, and whose testimony is rejected by the judge. The passage opens by stating that such a person is obligated to fast for Ramadan, based on Quran 2:185 ("So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it"). For Shawwal, he is not permitted to break fast, based on the hadith "Your fast is the day you fast and your breaking of fast is the day you break fast," meaning he must align with the community. The commentary clarifies that this applies regardless of whether the sky is cloudy or clear, and regardless of whether the person is a commoner or the imam himself. The imam does not command others to fast or break fast based on his private sighting. The passage then reports a differing view from al-Jawhara, where the imam may command fasting by his own sighting but still not lead Eid prayer or break fast openly. The commentator notes that the cautious approach (al-ih-tiyat) is adopted in both cases. A further quote from the author of the book (likely al-Quduri) states that if the imam is certain of the crescent, he may go out, pray Eid, and break fast. The commentator explains that the relied-upon view is the cautious one, to avoid error. The passage also discusses the implication of the hadith: while the general rule is to follow the community, a specific proof (the Quran verse) creates an exception for the individual. Regarding breaking fast on Shawwal, if he does break fast, he must make up the day but there is no expiation (kaffarah) because of the doubt (shubha) arising from his belief that it was Eid. The passage mentions a minority view that expiation is required. Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of combining textual proofs with caution (ihtiyat) in ambiguous situations.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): In this passage, 'adl' is defined as a person whose good deeds outweigh his bad deeds, and who has the quality of 'adalah' (integrity) which is a habit that leads to consistent piety and chivalry.
مستور (mastur): A person whose inner state is unknown; no apparent sin or integrity is known. In the context of hilal testimony, the mastur is accepted in the sahih opinion.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of a hadd penalty or expiation. In this passage, it refers to the doubt that arises because the person believes it is Eid based on his own sighting, so no expiation is due for breaking fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this commentary, what should an imam do if he is certain he saw the crescent of Shawwal alone and his testimony is rejected?
A) He should break his fast publicly and lead the Eid prayer.
B) He should continue fasting in secret and not lead the Eid prayer.
C) He should command the people to break fast but pray Eid alone.
D) He should ignore his sighting and break fast with the people.

Key Takeaway
Even with certainty of sighting, the Hanafi mufta bihi requires the individual—including the imam—to fast privately for Ramadan and to continue fasting for Shawwal until the community confirms Eid, to preserve unity and avoid error.

Ruling on Breaking Fast Based on Personal Moon Sighting
States ruling for one who sees the moon alone and breaks fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who breaks fast based on his own moon sighting and explain why no kaffarah is due.

Core Idea
A broken fast from personal sighting requires make-up but not expiation due to shubha.

What happens when a person sees the new moon of Ramadan or Shawwal alone and breaks his fast? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: the missed day must be made up (qada), but no expiatory fast (kaffarah) is required. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, focusing on the concept of shubha (doubt) and the evidence from Qur'an and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Pinpoint the exact scenario: a person sees the hilal alone and breaks fast. The ruling: qada wajib, no kaffarah.
The scenario applies to both Ramadan and Shawwal.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the evidence: Qur'an 2:185 (imperative to fast) and Hadith 'The fast is the day you all fast'.
The Hadith establishes communal practice as a factor.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the dalil prove qada? The imperative implies obligation; missing a wajib day requires make-up. Why no kaffarah? The Hadith creates a doubt (shubha) between personal certainty and public practice.
Shubha is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh that prevents penal expiation.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Some scholars argue for kaffarah because the act is outward violation of fasting. The Hanafi response: the inner belief that it is Eid creates shubha.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is no kaffarah.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The text states 'as-sahih' (the correct position) is no kaffarah. Later Hanafi authorities uphold this due to the strength of the shubha argument.
The tarjih is based on the principle that doubts prevent prescribed punishments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أفطر من رأى الهلال وحده في الوقتين رمضان وشوال قضى لما تلونا ولا كفارة عليه ولا على صديق للرائي إن شهد عنده بهلال الفطر وصدقه فافطر لأنه يوم عيد عنده فيكون شبهة وبرد شهادته في رمضان صار مكذباً شرعاً وبذلك لا كفارة عليه ولو كان فطره قبل ما رده القاضي في الصحيح لقيام الشبهة وهي قوله : الصوم يوم تصومون
And whoever breaks the fast having seen the new moon alone in either time (Ramadan or Shawwal) must make it up, as we have recited, and there is no expiation upon him, nor upon a friend of the seer if he testified before him regarding the new moon of breaking the fast and he believed him and thus broke his fast, because it is a day of Eid in his view, so it is a doubt; and his testimony is rejected regarding Ramadan, as he becomes legally a falsifier; and due to that there is no expiation upon him, even if he broke his fast before the judge rejected it in the correct view, due to the existence of the doubt, which is his statement: 'The fast is the day when you all fast.'
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing a specific scenario: a person who sees the new moon alone, whether for the start of Ramadan or for Shawwal (Eid al-Fitr), and then breaks his fast prematurely. The author (al-Tahtawi) first states the ruling: such a person must make up the missed fasting day (qada), referring to the Quranic verse 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185) and the prophetic hadith 'Begin fasting when you see it...'. However, there is no kaffarah (expiatory fasting or charity) upon him, nor upon a friend who, based on the seer's testimony for Eid, also broke his fast. The reason is that, in the seer's view, it is Eid, creating a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents kaffarah. For Ramadan, if the seer alone claims to have seen the crescent, his testimony is rejected because he becomes a falsifier according to the community's denial; still, no kaffarah is due because of the doubt. Even if the seer broke his fast before the judge rejected his testimony, the correct position holds no kaffarah due to this doubt, supported by the hadith 'The fast is the day when you all fast,' indicating that individual certainty does not legally obligate expiation when community practice contradicts it. The commentary explains that the proof is both the Quranic verse and the hadith, and that the doubt stems from the seer's personal conviction versus public action.

Key Terms
الهلال (al-hilal): The new crescent moon that marks the beginning of a lunar month, specifically Ramadan or Shawwal
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of a punitive or compensatory ruling (like kaffarah)
مكذباً شرعاً (mukadhdhiban shar'an): Legally considered a falsifier because the community's non-fasting contradicts his claim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person alone sees the new moon of Shawwal and breaks his fast. According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is required of him?
A) Qada only, no kaffarah
B) Qada and kaffarah
C) Only kaffarah, no qada
D) Neither qada nor kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Breaking fast based on personal moon sighting requires make-up (qada) but not expiation (kaffarah) because of the legal doubt (shubha) created by the conflict between personal certainty and communal practice.

Acceptance of a Single Witness for Ramadan Moon Sighting
Details acceptance conditions for a single witness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the Hanafi school accepts a single witness for the Ramadan moon sighting and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of an explanation.

Core Idea
A single upright witness suffices for Ramadan moon sighting – even a woman, slave, or repentant slanderer – because it is a religious matter.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi details the Hanafi ruling on accepting a single witness's testimony for the Ramadan moon when the sky is obstructed. It outlines the qualifications of the witness, the obligation to testify, and the concessions granted for women, slaves, and others. It also presents an internal Hanafi disagreement on whether the witness must explain how he saw the moon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its ruling
Determine when the ruling applies: when there is an obstruction in the sky (clouds, dust, fog, dew). The judge accepts the report of a single upright (adl) or concealed-state (mastur) witness.
The ruling is specific to Ramadan moon sighting.
2
List the qualifications of the witness
The witness can be adl (good deeds outweigh bad, with a quality of piety and manliness), mastur (unknown status – accepted in the correct view), a woman, a slave, or even a repentant slanderer (qadhf).
The mastur is accepted only when there is an obstruction, according to the correct view.
3
Understand the obligations and relaxations
The upright person is obliged to testify before the judge on the night of sighting. A secluded woman may testify without her guardian's permission. Formal wording of testimony (lafz shahada) and prior lawsuit are not required, by analogy to hadith narration.
This is because it is a religious matter, similar to transmitting hadith.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (Ibn al-Fadl) require the witness to explain how he saw the moon (e.g., 'I saw it enter the clouds and emerge') to remove the possibility of error. The general position (from al-Hidayah) accepts absolutely without explanation.
The relied-upon view is absolute acceptance, but Ibn al-Fadl's view is considered for precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان بالسماء علة من غيم أو غبار ونحوه كضباب وندى قبل أي القاضي بمجلسه خبر واحد عدل هو الذي حسناته أكثر من سيئاته، والعدالة ملكة تحمل على ملازمة التقوى والمروءة أو خبر مستور هو مجهول الحال لم يظهر له فسق ولا عدالة يقبل قوله في الصحيح ويلزم العدل أن يشهد عند الحاكم في ليلة رؤيته كي لا يصبحوا مفطرين وللمخدرة أن تشهد بغير إذن وليها لأنه من فروض العين ويقبل خبره لو شهد على شهادة واحد مثله لأن العدد في الأصول ليس بشرط فكذا في الفروع ويقبل خبره ولو كان أنثى، أو رقيقاً، أو محدوداً في قذف وقد تاب في ظاهر الرواية إثباتاً لرمضان لأنه أمر ديني وخير العدل فيه مقبول فأشبه رواية الأخبار ولهذا لا يشترط لفظ الشهادة ولا تقدم الدعوى كما لا يشترطان في سائر الأخبار، وأطلق القبول كما في الهداية وقال : كان الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : إنما يقبل شهادة الواحد إذا فسر فقال : رأيته في وقت يدخل في السحاب ثم ينجلي لأنّ الرؤية في مثل هذا تتفق في زمان قليل فجاز أن ينفرد هو به أما بدون هذا التفسير لا تقبل لمكان التهمة انتهى كذا في التجنيس . تنبيه لما كان قول الحساب مختلفاً فيه نظمه ابن وهبان فقال : وقول أولي التوقيـت لـيـس بـمـوجـب وقيل: نعم والبعض إن كان يكثر
And if there is an obstruction in the sky from clouds or dust or the like, such as fog and dew, the judge accepts in his court the report of a single upright person (adl) – one whose good deeds are more than his bad deeds, and uprightness is a quality that impels one to constant piety and manliness – or the report of a concealed-state person (mastur), who is of unknown status, not known to be sinful nor upright; his statement is accepted in the correct view. And it is incumbent upon the upright person to testify before the judge on the night of his sighting, so that they do not become breakfast. And a secluded woman may testify without the permission of her guardian because it is among the individual obligations. And his report is accepted if he testifies to the testimony of one like him, since number is not a condition in the principles, so likewise in the branches. And his report is accepted even if it is a woman, or a slave, or one who has been flogged for slander and has repented, in the apparent narration, for the establishment of Ramadan, because it is a religious matter and the report of the upright person therein is accepted, so it is analogous to the narration of reports. And for this reason, the wording of testimony is not required, nor the prior claim, as they are not required in other reports. And acceptance is absolute, as in al-Hidayah. And he said: The Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl used to say: The testimony of one person is only accepted if he explains, saying: 'I saw it at a time when it entered the cloud and then emerged,' because sighting in such a case occurs in a short time, so it is possible that he alone witnessed it. But without this explanation, it is not accepted due to accusation (of error). End quote from al-Tajnis. A note: Since the statement of the astronomers is disputed, Ibn Wahban composed: 'The statement of the timekeepers is not legally binding; some say yes, and some if they become numerous.'
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling statement on the acceptance of a single witness's testimony for the sighting of the new moon of Ramadan. It covers multiple conditions and exceptions. First, when the sky is cloudy, foggy, or dusty, the judge may accept the report of a single upright witness (adl) – defined as one whose good deeds outweigh bad, and who habitually practices piety and dignity – or even a 'mastur' witness (one whose moral status is unknown) in the correct view. The upright witness is obliged to testify before the judge on the night of sighting to enable the community to start fasting. A secluded woman can testify without her guardian's permission because this is a communal obligation that becomes individual (fard al-ayn) when no one else is available. Testimony can be received on the testimony of a single witness (shahada ala shahada) because numerical requirements are irrelevant in religious matters. The witness may be a woman, a slave, or even someone previously punished for false accusation (qadhf) provided he has repented – this is the position of the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). The reason is that Ramadan is a religious matter, akin to narration of hadith, where one trustworthy person suffices. Consequently, formal wording of testimony (lafz shahada) and prior lawsuit are not required. However, some Hanafi scholars like Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl conditioned acceptance on the witness explaining how he saw the moon, e.g., 'I saw it entering the clouds and then emerging,' to remove suspicion of error. Without such explanation, testimony is rejected due to possibility of mistake. The passage ends with a note that statements of astronomers (timekeepers) are not legally binding – a disputed point, with some accepting them if the astronomers are numerous.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): Person whose good deeds outweigh bad and who consistently practices piety and manliness (muru'a)
مستور (mastur): Person of unknown moral status – not known to be sinful nor upright; his report is accepted in the correct view for hilal
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): Report of a single person, accepted in religious matters like hilal and hadith narration

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition does the Hanafi madhhab accept the testimony of a 'mastur' witness for the Ramadan moon?
A) When the sky is cloudy
B) Always, without any condition
C) Only if the witness is a woman
D) Only if the witness explains how he saw it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a single witness for Ramadan moon sighting when the sky is obstructed, based on the analogy to hadith narration, with concessions for women, slaves, and repentant slanderers, though some Hanafis require an explanation.

Ruling on Astronomers' Statements for Moon Sighting
Dispute on relying on astronomers for crescent sighting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on reliance on astronomers for moon sighting and explain the conditions for testimony for crescent confirmation.

Core Idea
Astronomical calculations are not relied upon for moon sighting; visual testimony is required.

Determining the start of Ramadan and Eid al-Fitr hinges on moon sighting. While astronomical calculations offer precision, the Hanafi school, along with the majority of Shafi'is, insists on actual visual testimony. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this position and the conditions for accepting such testimony.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that astronomers' statements are not relied upon for moon sighting according to the Hanafi school.
This is the default position from the Hanafi texts.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that some later Shafi'is, like Taqi al-Din al-Subki, argued that astronomical calculation is definitive and should be accepted.
This is a minority view within the Shafi'i school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opposing argument: calculation is qat'i (definitive) and does not fall under the prohibition of soothsaying.
The opponent cites the certainty of mathematical astronomy.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi counter-argument: the default in ritual matters is to avoid calculation; the Quranic verse (10:5) refers to general knowledge, not religious fixing.
Hanafis maintain that visual sighting is the established practice.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafis do not accept calculations for beginning or ending Ramadan, while Subki would.
This affects millions of Muslims in practice.

Ibn al-Shihnah said after reporting the disagreement: Thus the companions of Abu Hanifah, except for a rare one, and al-Shafi'i agreed that there is no reliance on the statements of astrologers in this matter. And the condition for the crescent of breaking the fast, i.e., its confirmation and the confirmation of other crescents, if there is a cloud in the sky, is the word 'testimony' given by two free Muslim men of legal capacity, not hadd-punished for slander, or one man and two women, but without the condition of a prior claim for testimony, like the freeing of a slave woman or the divorce of a wife. If someone sees the crescent in a village and there is no governor or judge, then the people may fast on his statement, and for breaking the fast, if two just men inform of the sighting with a cloudy sky, there is no harm for them to break the fast without a claim or a ruling due to necessity. And if there is no cloud in the sky, then for confirmation, a large group is necessary for Ramadan and for breaking the fast, and for others, because the place of observation is the same, obstacles are absent, eyesight is sound, and the aspirations for seeking the crescent are upright. So lone testimony in such a situation is likely to cause error. Some scholars, including Muhammad ibn Salama, considered that if he asks them and relies on their statement after a group agrees among themselves...
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a dispute regarding the reliance on astronomers for moon sighting. The author begins by quoting Ibn al-Shihnah, who states that the majority of Hanafi scholars (except a rare one) and Imam al-Shafi'i agree that astronomers' statements are not to be relied upon. However, some later Shafi'i scholars like Taqi al-Din al-Subki inclined to rely on them because astronomical calculations are definitive. The Hanafi response is that trusting such calculations is not like consulting a soothsayer; the Quran itself mentions the moon's phases for calculating time. The passage then discusses the conditions for testimony for the crescent of Eid al-Fitr and other crescents: when the sky is cloudy, the testimony of two free Muslim men (or one man and two women) is sufficient. But if the sky is clear, a large group is required because individual testimony is prone to error. There is also a discussion on the requirement of a prior claim; for matters like freeing a slave woman or divorce, no prior claim is needed. In villages (rustaq) without a judge, if a just person reports sighting, people can act on it due to necessity. This lesson focuses on the first part: the ruling on astronomers.

Key Terms
المنجمين (al-munajjimin): Astronomers who calculate lunar months based on mathematical methods
الهلال (al-hilal): The crescent moon that marks the beginning of a lunar month
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony, the legal declaration of sighting
الدعوى (al-da'wa): Legal claim or lawsuit that precedes testimony in some cases

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is obligatory to follow them
B) They are not relied upon
C) They are exclusively relied upon
D) It is recommended but not necessary

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not rely on astronomical calculations for moon sighting; visual testimony from just witnesses is required, with conditions varying by weather and availability of judges.

Conditions for Testimony in Moon Sighting
Specification of witness requirements for crescent testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid testimony of crescent sighting and distinguish between clear and cloudy sky requirements.

Core Idea
Testimony for the crescent requires two just men, or one man two women, but in cloudy skies two men suffice; in clear skies a large crowd is needed.

The sighting of the new moon determines the beginning and end of Ramadan. The Hanafi school prescribes specific conditions for such testimony to establish reliability. This passage from al-Tahtawi's commentary details those conditions, drawing from the principle that the benefit (al-fitr) affects the people, so testimony must meet the same standards as other rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the standard witnesses required for crescent testimony: two free Muslim men of legal capacity, not hadd-punished for qadhf, or one man and two women.
2
Note the exception for prior claim
Understand that no prior claim (da'wa) is needed for this testimony, similar to cases like freeing a slave or divorce.
3
Differentiate sky conditions
Distinguish between cloudy and clear sky: in cloudy conditions, two just men suffice; in clear conditions, a large group (jamm' adhim) is required.
4
Rationale for large group
The reason (talil) for requiring a large group in clear skies: the place of observation is the same, obstacles are absent, eyesight is sound, and aspirations are upstanding, so lone testimony would likely cause error.
5
Necessity exception in villages
In a village without a judge or governor, a single just person's report is accepted out of necessity — for fasting or breaking fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage states the conditions for testimony regarding the crescent. The normal requirement is two free Muslim men of legal capacity who have not been punished for slander (qadhf). Alternatively, one man and two women suffice, but without the requirement of a prior claim (da'wa). This is because the benefit is for the people (ibad), similar to other rights. For cloudy sky, two just men are enough. For clear sky, a large crowd is needed to avoid error. In villages where no judge is present, a single just person's report is accepted for fasting or breaking fast due to necessity.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-adalah): Moral probity; the witness must be known for uprightness
الرستاق (al-rustaq): Village or rural area without a judge or governor

Key Takeaway

Breaking Fast After 30 Days Without Crescent Sighting
Determining whether to break fast when 30 days completed but no sighting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking the fast after completing 30 days of Ramadan when the Shawwal crescent is not sighted, and identify the internal dispute regarding the number of witnesses required.

Core Idea
If the sky is clear, do not break the fast after 30 days—non-sighting indicates error; if cloudy, break.

This lesson explores a critical scenario for the end of Ramadan: when 30 days have passed but the Shawwal crescent is not sighted. The author distinguishes between clear and cloudy skies, revealing an internal Hanafi dispute about the weight of testimony and the permissibility of breaking the fast. The ruling also addresses ta'zir for false witnessing and extends to Eid al-Adha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the status of the sky (clear or cloudy) and how Ramadan was established (single witness or two witnesses).
The ruling differs based on these variables.
2
State the ruling for clear sky with single witness
If Ramadan was proven by one witness and the sky is clear with no sighting, breaking fast is not permissible (agreeably). The witness is subject to ta'zir.
This is based on al-Durar and al-Tajnis.
3
Analyze the dispute for clear sky with two witnesses
When two witnesses established Ramadan and the sky is clear, Hanafis differ: some allow breaking (testimony equals sighting), while others forbid (non-sighting reveals error).
Al-Zayla'i and Nasir al-Din side with forbidding.
4
Apply the cloudy sky exception
If the sky is cloudy, breaking the fast is unanimously permissible regardless of the number of witnesses, because error is not apparent.
No disagreement among Hanafis here.
5
Extend to Eid al-Adha and internal dispute
For Eid al-Adha, the ruling mirrors Eid al-Fitr: a large group is needed for clear sky (apparent narration), unlike a single witness (narrated from Abu Hanifa in al-Nawadir). The madhhab prefers the apparent narration.
The mufta bihi requires a large group for clear sky.

Source Text

وإذا تم العدد أي عدد رمضان ثلاثين (بشهادة فرد) برؤيته (ولم ير هلال الفطر و ذلك والسماء مصحية لا يحل الفطر) اتفاقاً على ما ذكره شمس ويعزر ذلك الشاهد كذا في الدرر وفي التجنيس إذا لم ير هلال شوال لا يفطرون حتى يصوموا يوماً آخر، وقال الزيلعي والأشبه أن يقال إن كانت السماء مصحية لا يفطرون لظهور غلطه وإن كانت متغيمة يفطرون لعدم ظهور الغلط واختلف الترجيح) في حل الفطر (فيما إذا كان) ثبوت رمضان بشهادة عدلين وتم العدد، ولم ير هلال شوال مع الصحو صحيح في الدراية والخلاصة والبززية حل الفطر لأن شهادة الشاهدين إذا قبلت كانت بمنزلة العيان، وفي مجموع النوازل لا يفطرون وصححه كذلك السيد الإمام الأجل ناصر الدين لأن عدم الرؤية مع الصحو دليل الغلط فتبطل شهادتهم (ولا خلاف في حل الفطر إذا تم العدد وكان بالسماء علة ولو) وصلية (ثبت رمضان بشهادة الفرد) العدل كالعدلين اتفاقاً على التحقيق وهلال الأضحى) في الحكم (كالفطر) فلا بد من نصاب مع العلة، والجمع العظيم مع الصحو على ظاهر الرواية، وهو الأصح لما تعلق به من نفع العباد خلافاً لما يروى عن أبي حنيفة أنه كهلال رمضان، وهي رواية النوادر وصححها في التحفة، والمذهب ظاهر الرواية (ويشترط) في الثبوت (لبقية الأهلة) إذا كان بالسماء علة (شهادة رجلين عدلين (أو شهادة حرو حرتين غير محدودين في قذف وإلا
And if the number (i.e., the number of Ramadan) is completed to thirty by the testimony of an individual who saw it, and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen while the sky is clear, it is not permissible to break the fast, according to agreement, as Shams mentioned, and that witness is to be punished (ta'zir). This is in al-Durar and al-Tajnis: if the crescent of Shawwal is not seen, they do not break fast until they fast another day. Al-Zayla'i said: the more likely is: if the sky is clear, they do not break fast because his error is apparent; if it is cloudy, they break fast because the error is not apparent. There is a difference in preference regarding the permissibility of breaking the fast when Ramadan is established by two just witnesses, the number is completed, and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen with clear sky. In al-Diraya, al-Khulasa, and al-Bazzazi, it is considered permissible to break fast because the testimony of two witnesses, once accepted, is equivalent to ocular sighting. In Majmu' al-Nawazil, they do not break fast, and this was verified by the chief imam Nasir al-Din, because non-sighting with clear sky is evidence of error, so their testimony is invalid. There is no disagreement about the permissibility of breaking fast if the number is completed and the sky is cloudy, even if Ramadan was established by the testimony of a single just person, just like two witnesses, unanimously upon investigation. The crescent of Adha (Eid al-Adha) is similar in ruling to that of breaking the fast (Eid al-Fitr): a full complement is needed when cloudy, and a large group when clear, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), and it is the most correct because it involves benefit for the servants. This is contrary to what is narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is like the crescent of Ramadan, which is a narrated opinion from al-Nawadir and preferred in al-Tuhfa, but the madhhab is the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute regarding breaking the fast after completing 30 days of Ramadan. If the month was established by a single witness, and after 30 days the Shawwal crescent is not seen with a clear sky, it is agreed that breaking fast is not allowed, and the witness is punished. But if established by two witnesses, there is a dispute: some say break fast because two witnesses are like actual sighting; others say do not break because non-sighting in clear sky indicates error. The preferred view is to break if cloudy, not if clear. The ruling for Eid al-Adha is similar, but there is a difference: according to the apparent narration, it follows the rules of Eid al-Fitr (requiring a large group for clear sky), but some narrations from Abu Hanifa say it is like Ramadan (single witness suffices). The madhhab (mufta bihi) is the apparent narration.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment given by the judge for the false witness
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known narrations from the founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad)
بقية الأهلة (baqiyyat al-ahillah): The remaining crescents (other than Ramadan and Shawwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-mu'tamad), if Ramadan was established by two witnesses and 30 days complete but the Shawwal crescent is not seen with a clear sky, do the people break fast?
A) Yes, they break
B) No, they do not break
C) It is recommended to break
D) It is optional

Key Takeaway
If 30 days of Ramadan pass and the Shawwal crescent is not sighted, break fast only if the sky is cloudy; if clear, fast an additional day.

Testimony Requirements for Other Crescents
Stating witness conditions for other moon sightings besides Ramadan and Eid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the witness requirements for confirming lunar months other than Ramadan and the two Eids when the sky is cloudy.

Core Idea
Two just men or one man and two women are required for other crescents.

This lesson focuses on the conditions for confirming the sighting of the crescent moon for months other than Ramadan and the two Eids. The Hanafi school distinguishes between months: for Ramadan and Eid, a large group suffices even if cloudy, but for other months, a specific pair of witnesses is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is confirming the sighting of the crescent for a month other than Ramadan or Eid.
2
Note the condition
The ruling applies when the sky is cloudy (ʿilla).
If the sky is clear, different rules apply (not covered in this passage).
3
State the witness composition
Two just men, or one man and two just women, must testify. They must not have been hadd-punished for slander.
This mirrors the standard witness requirement from Quran 2:282.
4
Understand the wisdom
The stricter requirement is because the benefit of knowing other months is less urgent than Ramadan and Eid.
The large crowd exception only applies to Ramadan and Eid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط) في الثبوت (لبقية الأهلة) إذا كان بالسماء علة (شهادة رجلين عدلين (أو شهادة حرو حرتين غير محدودين في قذف وإلا
And it is required for the confirmation of other crescents, if there is a cloud in the sky, the testimony of two just men or the testimony of one man and two women who have not been hadd-punished for slander.
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This short passage gives the general rule for all other crescents (like the month of Hajj, etc.). If the sky is cloudy, the testimony of two just men or one man and two women is required. This is similar to the rules for Ramadan but without the exception of a large group because for other months the benefit to people is less direct, so the stricter condition of two witnesses applies.

Key Terms
بقية الأهلة (baqiyat al-ahillah): The remaining lunar months other than Ramadan and the two Eids

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
For other lunar months, two just men or one man and two women are required when the sky is cloudy.

Requirement of a Large Crowd for Moon Sighting
Intra-Hanafi dispute on moon sighting witness numbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the required number of witnesses for moon sighting in clear weather and identify the mufta bihi opinion that delegates the matter to the Imam's discretion.

Core Idea
The number of witnesses for moon sighting is not fixed; the Imam decides based on circumstances.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a key question in Hanafi fiqh: How many witnesses are needed to confirm the sighting of the crescent moon when the sky is clear? The Hanafi school recognizes that error is more likely from a small number, so caution requires a large multitude. However, scholars differed on the exact number. The passage reviews several opinions and concludes that the matter is delegated to the Imam's judgment, as times, places, and witness reliability vary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage deals with the requirement of a large crowd for moon sighting specifically when the sky is clear, to avoid error from a few witnesses.
2
List the Hanafi opinions
Note the various positions: Abu Yusuf: 50 (like qasama); Khalaf: 500 in Balkh is few; al-Baqqali: 1000 in Bukhara is few; Muhammad: consider widespread transmission; another narration from Muhammad: delegate to Imam's discretion.
These are all intra-Hanafi views.
3
Analyze the principle behind each opinion
Understand why each scholar chose that number: Abu Yusuf analogized to qasama, others relied on local custom, and the delegation view is based on variability of circumstances.
4
Determine the correct position
The text states that the delegation view is the correct (mufta bihi) position because circumstances vary and people differ in truthfulness.
This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, the Imam can accept a smaller number if he deems the witnesses truthful, without being bound by a fixed count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغلط فوجب التوقف في رؤية القليل حتى يراه الجمع الكثير لا فرق في ظاهر الرواية بين أهل المصر ومن ورد من خارج المصر ومقدار عدد الجمع العظيم قيل: أهل المحلة، وعن أبي يوسف خمسون كالقسامة وعن خلف خمسمائة ببلخ قليل، وقال البقالي: الألف بخاري قليل وقال الكمال: الحق ما روي عن محمد وأبي يوسف أن العبرة لتواتر الخبر ومجيئه من كل جانب انتهى وفي التجنيس عن محمد أن أمر القلة والكثرة مفوض إلى رأي الإمام وهو الصحيح وفي البرهان في الأصح لأن ذلك يختلف باختلاف الأوقات والأماكن وتتفاوت الناس صدقاً. قوله: (مفوض إلى رأي الإمام) من غير تقدير بعدد كما في التنوير. قوله: (وتتفاوت الناس صدقاً) أي من جهة الصدق أي فيمكن أن يغلب صدق بعض الناس عنده، فيقبله.
Error [is likely], so it is necessary to pause regarding the sighting by a small [number] until a large multitude sees it. There is no difference in the apparent narration between the people of the city and those coming from outside. The quantity of the great multitude: it is said the people of the neighborhood. From Abu Yusuf: fifty, like the oath (qasama). From Khalaf: five hundred in Balkh is few. Al-Baqqali said: a thousand in Bukhara is few. Al-Kamal said: the truth is what was narrated from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf that consideration is given to the widespread transmission of the report and its coming from every side. End. In al-Tajnis from Muhammad: the matter of few and many is delegated to the opinion of the Imam, and that is correct. In al-Burhan: it is the most correct because it varies with times and places, and people differ in truthfulness. His saying: (delegated to the opinion of the Imam) without specifying a number, as in al-Tanwir. His saying: (and people differ in truthfulness) meaning in terms of truthfulness, so it is possible that the truthfulness of some people may prevail in his estimation, so he accepts it.
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses the number of witnesses required for moon sighting when the sky is clear. Because error is likely from few witnesses, caution requires relying on a large multitude. The apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwaya) makes no distinction between residents and outsiders. Various scholars specified different numbers: the people of the neighborhood, fifty (by Abu Yusuf), five hundred in Balkh (by Khalaf), one thousand in Bukhara (by al-Baqqali). However, al-Kamal preferred the view of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf that the criterion is widespread and multi-directional transmission. Another narration from Muhammad delegates the matter to the Imam's discretion, which is the correct position as it varies with circumstances. The commentary explains that no fixed number is set, and the Imam may judge based on the perceived truthfulness of the witnesses.

Key Terms
الجمع العظيم (al-jam' al-'azim): A large multitude of people whose sighting of the crescent is considered reliable.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings of the Hanafi school from the six books of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct (mufta bihi) position regarding the number of witnesses for moon sighting in clear weather?
A) Fixed number of fifty witnesses
B) Fixed number of five hundred
C) Delegated to Imam's opinion
D) Two witnesses suffice

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not fix a number for moon sighting witnesses; the Imam decides based on circumstances.

Breaking Fast When Shawwal Crescent Not Sighted in Clear Sky
Examines whether to break fast on Eid when Ramadan completes 30 days but no moon sighting in clear sky

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding breaking the fast when Ramadan completes 30 days but the Shawwal crescent is not sighted in a clear sky, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf of each position.

Core Idea
When the sky is clear, the absence of a crescent sighting can invalidate witness testimony, sparking a Hanafi split.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in the fiqh of fasting: after completing 30 days of Ramadan, the Shawwal crescent is not seen despite a clear sky. The ruling on whether to break fast for Eid depends on how we weight the original testimony that established Ramadan. The Hanafi school contains two distinct positions, each with strong reasoning. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how magistrates weigh conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine the facts: Ramadan proven by two witnesses, 30 days completed, Shawwal not sighted in clear sky. The question is whether the fast may be broken.
Note the conditional: clear sky vs cloudy changes the analysis.
2
State the first position
Some Hanafi sources (al-Dirayah, al-Khulasah, al-Bazzaziyah) allow breaking fast. Their proof: accepted testimony is like direct sight (bimanzilat al-'iyan).
This position treats the testimony as certain.
3
State the second position
Other sources (Majmu' al-Nawazil) forbid breaking fast. Their proof: the clear sky absence indicates error, so the testimony is invalidated.
This position treats the empirical evidence as overriding.
4
Analyse the proofs
Contrast the two proofs: testimony-as-certainty vs. reality-as-certainty. Consider how each uses the same fact (clear sky) differently.
The dispute is methodological: which indicator is stronger?
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical consequence: some judges would permit Eid, others require an extra fasting day. The mufta bihi is not explicitly settled for this scenario, but the analogous Adha ruling prefers the cautious view (Zahir al-Riwayah).
The public benefit (naf' al-'ibad) supports the stricter view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تم العدد أي عدد رمضان ثلاثين بشهادة فرد برؤيته ولم ير هلال الفطر وذلك والسماء مصحية لا يحل الفطر اتفاقاً على ما ذكره شمس ويعزر ذلك الشاهد كذا في الدرر وفي التجنيس إذا لم ير هلال شوال لا يفطرون حتى يصوموا يوماً آخر، وقال الزيلعي والأشبه أن يقال إن كانت السماء مصحية لا يفطرون لظهور غلطه وإن كانت متغيمة يفطرون لعدم ظهور الغلط واختلف الترجيح في حل الفطر فيما إذا كان ثبوت رمضان بشهادة عدلين وتم العدد، ولم ير هلال شوال مع الصحو صحيح في الدراية والخلاصة والبزازية حل الفطر لأن شهادة الشاهدين إذا قبلت كانت بمنزلة العيان، وفي مجموع النوازل لا يفطرون وصححه كذلك السيد الإمام الأجل ناصر الدين لأن عدم الرؤية مع الصحو دليل الغلط فتبطل شهادتهم ولا خلاف في حل الفطر إذا تم العدد وكان بالسماء علة ولو ثبت رمضان بشهادة الفرد العدل كالعدلين اتفاقاً على التحقيق وهلال الأضحى في الحكم كالفطر فلا بد من نصاب مع العلة، والجمع العظيم مع الصحو على ظاهر الرواية، وهو الأصح لما تعلق به من نفع العباد خلافاً لما يروى عن أبي حنيفة أنه كهلال رمضان، وهي رواية النوادر وصححها في التحفة، والمذهب ظاهر الرواية
And when the [month's] count is completed, i.e. the count of Ramadan reaches thirty, based on the testimony of one individual that he saw it, and [then] the crescent of Shawwal is not seen, and that [is] while the sky is clear, it is not permissible to break the fast by consensus, as mentioned by Shams, and that witness is to be disciplined (ta'zir). Similarly in al-Tajnis: if the crescent of Shawwal is not seen, they do not break fast until they fast an additional day. Al-Zayla'i said: the more likely view is that if the sky is clear, they do not break fast because his error is apparent; if it is cloudy, they break fast because error is not apparent. And there is a difference in weighting regarding the permissibility of breaking fast when the month of Ramadan was established by the testimony of two just witnesses and the count is completed, and the crescent of Shawwal was not seen while the sky is clear. The position in al-Dirayah, al-Khulasah, and al-Bazzaziyah is that it is permissible to break fast because the testimony of two witnesses, when accepted, is equivalent to direct observation. In Majmu' al-Nawazil, they do not break fast, and this was affirmed by the Sayyid Imam al-Ajal Nasir al-Din because the absence of sighting with a clear sky is evidence of error, so their testimony is invalidated. There is no disagreement on the permissibility of breaking fast when the count is completed and the sky is cloudy, even if Ramadan was established by the testimony of a single just witness, like two witnesses, by agreement upon verification. And the crescent of Adha (Dhul-Hijjah) in ruling is like the crescent of Shawwal (Eid al-Fitr): thus a [minimum] number [of witnesses] is required with cloud cover, and a great multitude with clear sky, according to the apparent narration, and it is the most correct because it is connected to the benefit of the servants, contrary to what is narrated from Abu Hanifah that it is like the crescent of Ramadan, which is a narration from al-Nawadir, and it was confirmed in al-Tuhfah, but the madhhab is the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex scenario when Ramadan has been completed to 30 days based on a witness, but the Shawwal crescent is not sighted on the 30th night. There is consensus that if the sky is clear, breaking fast is not allowed and the witness should be punished (ta'zir). Al-Zayla'i differentiates: in clear sky, don't break; if cloudy, break. A key intra-Hanafi dispute arises when Ramadan was proven by two witnesses and the sky is clear: some authorities (al-Dirayah, al-Khulasah, al-Bazzaziyah) allow breaking fast, equating accepted testimony to direct sight; while others (Majmu' al-Nawazil, endorsed by Sayyid Imam Nasir al-Din) forbid it due to the clear-sky absence being evidence of error. The passage then notes the crescent of Adha is treated like Shawwal: cloudy requires a specific number, clear requires a great multitude. This is the preferred view (Zahir al-Riwayah), despite a Nawadir narration from Abu Hanifa treating it like Ramadan. The commentary explains the rationale: the requirement of numerous witnesses for Adha is tied to public benefit, like financial rights.

Key Terms
شهادة عدلين (shahadat 'adlayn): Testimony of two just male witnesses.
الرواية النوادر (al-riwayah al-nawadir): Rare or less well-known transmissions within the Hanafi school, as opposed to Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the sky is clear and Ramadan was proven by two witnesses, what is the ruling on breaking fast after completing 30 days without seeing Shawwal?
A) Unanimously permissible
B) Unanimously impermissible
C) There is a dispute; some allow, some disallow
D) It is permissible only if the witnesses are from outside

Key Takeaway
When the sky is clear, the failure to sight the Shawwal crescent creates a legitimate intra-Hanafi split on whether to break fast, with the practical consequence of an extra fasting day if the cautious view is followed.

Witness Requirements for Non-Ramadan Months
Sets witness conditions for non-Ramadan months

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the witness conditions for establishing the new moon for months other than Ramadan in the Hanafi school, distinguishing between cloudy and clear skies.

Core Idea
Witness requirement depends on sky clarity: two men (or one man+two women) if cloudy; a multitude if clear.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on crescent sighting for months other than Ramadan. While Ramadan’s crescent can be established by a single just witness, other months demand more rigorous proof. The text from Maraqi al-Falah specifies distinct conditions based on whether the sky is clear or obscured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is establishing the beginning of a lunar month (other than Ramadan) based on moon sighting. The ruling is that certain witness conditions must be met for the month to be considered validly begun.
This is a shart (condition) for the validity of the month's start.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The condition varies depending on whether the sky has an 'illah (cloud cover, dust, etc.) or is clear (safwa).
The term 'illah here means any atmospheric obstruction.
3
Apply the condition for cloudy sky
If the sky is cloudy, the testimony of two just men (rajulayn 'adlayn) or one free man and two free women (hurr wa hurratayn) is required.
The women must be free and not convicted of qadhf (false accusation of adultery).
4
Apply the condition for clear sky
If the sky is clear, a great multitude (jama' 'azim) is required. No exact number is specified; it must be a crowd that normally makes sighting certain.
This is stricter because sighting is easier when the sky is clear.
5
Note the underlying rationale
The difference is based on the ease of sighting: when the sky is clear, many people should have seen it; when cloudy, fewer reliable witnesses suffice.
This shows Hanafi attention to practical reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط في الثبوت لبقية الأهلة إذا كان بالسماء علة شهادة رجلين عدلين أو شهادة حرو حرتين غير محدودين في قذف وإلا فجمع عظيم
And it is a condition for the establishment [of the new moon] for the rest of the crescents, if there is a cover (cloudiness) in the sky, the testimony of two just men or the testimony of a free man and two free women who are not convicted of false accusation (qadhf), and if not [i.e., if the sky is clear], then a great multitude.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This concise ruling from the matn (Maraqi al-Falah) as commented upon by al-Tahtawi states the witness requirements for all months other than Ramadan. When the sky is cloudy, the sighting must be confirmed by two just male witnesses, or one male and two female witnesses (provided none have been punished for false accusation of adultery). When the sky is clear, a large multitude is required, as explained in previous lessons, without specifying an exact number. This rule applies to months like Shawwal, Dhul-Hijjah, and others. The commentary (not included in this extract) elaborates on the rationale.

Key Terms
بقية الأهلة (baqiyat al-ahillah): All lunar months except Ramadan.
علة ('illah): Atmospheric cover (clouds, dust, etc.) preventing clear sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for establishing a new moon for months other than Ramadan when the sky is cloudy?
A) One just male witness
B) Two just men or one man and two women
C) A large multitude
D) Any two witnesses regardless of gender

Key Takeaway
For non-Ramadan months, cloudy skies require two men or one man and two women; clear skies require a multitude.

8 min
Binding Nature of Moon Sighting Across Localities
Explains whether moon sighting in one city obligates fasting in other cities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dominant Hanafi position that moon sighting in one locality obligates all Muslims to fast, explain the proof from the general command, and distinguish the minority view based on ikhtilaf al-matali'.

Core Idea
A valid sighting anywhere triggers fasting everywhere — the command is universal, not local.

The crescent moon of Ramadan is a shared signal for the entire Muslim community. But if it is seen in one city, must Muslims in another city also begin fasting? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on this question, which has important practical implications for global unity and individual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage addresses whether the sighting of the crescent in one town obligates fasting upon Muslims in other towns.
The central legal question is the scope of the obligation.
2
State the dominant Hanafi position
The apparent madhhab (zahir al-madhab) holds that it is binding on all people, and this is the basis of fatwa.
Also the saying of most mashayikh (scholars).
3
Locate the proof
The proof is the general address 'Fast upon seeing it' (صوموا لرؤيته) from the Quran or hadith.
The command is universal and not restricted to the place of sighting.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'sūmū' indicates obligation; the phrase 'li-ru'yatihi' is general, so any valid sighting triggers the duty for all addressed.
In Hanafi usul, a general command (amr mutlaq) is binding on all instances.
5
Note the minority view and its refutation
Some scholars (e.g., author of al-Tajrid) argue that sighting differs by rising place, likening it to prayer times. However, the dominant view rejects this analogy and reaffirms the general command.
The minority view is mentioned but is not the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت الهلال في بلدة ومطلع قطرها لزم سائر الناس في ظاهر المذهب وعليه الفتوى وهو قول أكثر المشايخ: فيلزم قضاء على أهل بلدة صاموا تسعة وعشرين يوماً لعموم الخطاب صوموا لرؤيته وقيل يختلف ثبوته باختلاف المطالع، واختاره صاحب التجريد وغيره كما إذا زالت الشمس عند قوم وغربت عند غيرهم فالظهر على الأولين لا المغرب لعدم انعقاد السبب في حقهم.
And when the crescent is established in a town and the rising place of its climate zone, it becomes binding upon all people in the apparent madhhab, and upon it is the fatwa, and it is the saying of most mashayikh: thus the people of a town who fasted twenty-nine days must make up a day, because of the generality of the address, 'Fast upon seeing it.' And it is said that its establishment differs according to different rising places, and this was chosen by the author of al-Tajrid and others, like when the sun has set for some people and set for others; then the zuhr prayer is upon the first, not maghrib, because the cause has not occurred in their case.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage from the hashya discusses a contested issue: whether moon sighting in one locale obligates fasting in all other locales. The dominant Hanafi position (apparent madhhab, adopted by most scholars, and the basis for fatwa) is that it is binding on all. They argue based on the general command 'Fast upon seeing it' — the command is universal, so if any Muslims see it, all must fast. A practical consequence: if those in a city fast 29 days based on the general sighting but later realize they missed a day, they must make it up. However, a minority view (chosen by the author of al-Tajrid) holds that moon sighting differs by rising place (matla'), just as prayer times differ. The example: if sunset differs between two locations, the maghrib prayer is not due on those for whom the cause (sunset) hasn't occurred. By analogy, moon sighting in one place does not bind another. The commentary notes that the first opinion is the established fatwa.

Key Terms
مطلع القطر (matla' al-qutr): The rising point of the crescent specific to a climate zone or region.
ظاهر المذهب (zahir al-madhab): The well-known and dominant position of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dominant Hanafi position (apparent madhhab), if the crescent is sighted in one town, what is the ruling for Muslims in other towns?
A) They are not obligated to fast until they see it themselves
B) They are obligated to fast even if they did not see it
C) They fast but do not need to make up if they miss a day
D) The ruling depends on the distance between towns

Key Takeaway
When the crescent is sighted in any town, all Muslims are obligated to fast based on the general command, and if they fasted 29 days based on that sighting, they must make up a missed day.

Ruling on Moon Sighting and Its Binding Across Localities
Explains the binding effect of moon sighting within the same matla' and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the binding of moon sighting across localities and apply it to determine the obligation of fasting or making up fasts.

Core Idea
Moon sighting in one locality binds all Muslims of the same rising place (matla') in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Might moon sighting in one city require everyone worldwide to fast? Or does geography matter? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling: the general hadith 'Fast by seeing it' is balanced with practical differences in moonrise. You'll learn the relied-upon view, the evidence, and an important intra-madhhab dispute that affects how we apply the rule today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Locate where the text states the relied-upon view (zahir al-madhhab) on binding of moon sighting. Note the condition: 'in a town and its rising place (matla')'.
The phrase 'لزم سائر الناس في ظاهر المذهب' indicates the binding.
2
Analyze the proof
Find the hadith 'صوموا لرؤيته' and understand its wajh al-dalalah: the imperative indicates obligation, and 'رؤيته' is absolute, so any valid sighting triggers the obligation for all.
The proof is ahad but clear in meaning (qat'i dalala).
3
Examine the intra-madhhab dispute
The author of al-Tajrid holds that sighting does not bind those with different matali', analogizing prayer times. Compare the two views and note which is preferred (asah vs ashbah).
Minority view is 'ashbah' but the majority is 'asah' and mufta bihi.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the ruling to the scenario: If a town fasts 29 days and later learns that sighting was established elsewhere, must they make up a day? Yes, per the relied-upon view.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Review procedural conditions
Note additional details: sighting must be established by two witnesses, widespread news, or judge's ruling; sighting during the day is disregarded; Abu Yusuf's view on pre-noon sighting.
These refine the application.

A great multitude (and when the crescent is sighted in a town and its rising place, it obligates all people in the apparent madhhab, and upon it is the fatwa, and it is the saying of most scholars: so it necessitates making up the fast for people of a town who fasted twenty-nine days due to the generality of the address 'Fast by its sighting.' His saying: (and the rising of its region) it is better to say: and when the crescent is established in the rising of the region, etc. His saying: (obligates all people) in all regions of the world if the sighting is established by a binding method, such as two people bearing witness, or they testify to the judge's ruling, or the news is widespread, contrary to if he informs that the people of such a town saw it, because it is a report. His saying: (Fast by its sighting) is a substitute for the address, for he tied fasting to absolute sighting, which is achieved by the sighting of a group, so the generality of the ruling is established as a precaution. His saying: (and the author of al-Tajrid chose it) and it is more appropriate, even if the first is more correct, as in al-Sayyid. His saying: (like when the sun sets, etc.) He said in Sharh al-Sayyid: because the separation of the crescent from the sun's rays differs by regions, like the entry and exit of time, so that if the sun rises in the east, it does not necessitate that it rises in the west, and likewise the dawn and sunset, but as it moves a degree, that is dawn for a people and dawn for others, and sunset for some, and midnight for others, and this is established in astronomy and the science of the celestial spheres. The least difference in rising places is a month's journey, as in al-Jawahir, based on the story of Sulayman (peace be upon him), for he traveled every morning and evening from one region to another, and between each was a month's journey. His saying: (by something like a suspended agency) such that a person claims against another debtor that the creditor said: 'If Ramadan or Shawwal comes, then I have appointed you as agent to collect the debt I have from so-and-so.' The debtor acknowledges the debt and the agency but denies the entry of Ramadan or Shawwal. If this is true, then the matter is clear; if false, then the justification is to establish the right of the Lawgiver in Ramadan or the creation in breaking the fast. Apparently, it pays attention to the madhhab of the two companions who say that a prior claim is not required. His saying: (in the news of a great multitude) means many people who inform of something like sighting the crescent, and it does not mean two people if the judge sees that. His saying: (No consideration is given to sighting the crescent during the day) means no consideration is given to it from the past night, but rather for the coming night. His saying: (from it) means from us, due to a hadith. His saying: (at the evening of each month) means if it is seen at the evening of the night, then the coming night is from it, and this is that if it is seen before noon. Abu Yusuf makes the crescent seen before noon for the past night in fasting and breaking the fast, and there are other sayings mentioned in al-Sharh. And Allah knows best. I seek forgiveness from Allah the Great.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of Maraqi al-Falah on the ruling of moon sighting. It opens by presenting the relied-upon view of the Hanafi madhhab (zahir al-madhhab) that if the moon is sighted in one locality that shares a single rising place (matla') with another, the ruling binds all Muslims. The proof is the general address 'Fast by its sighting' from the hadith, which implies that sighting by anyone necessitates fasting for all. However, there is a minority view, chosen by the author of al-Tajrid and others, that sighting differs by region, similar to differences in sunrise and sunset where zuhr is not binding on those who have not yet reached that time. The commentary elaborates on the conditions for the ruling to be binding (e.g., testimony by two witnesses, widespread news) and distinguishes it from mere report. It also discusses the establishment of Ramadan and Shawwal through judicial claim, noting a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): according to the Imam, a prior claim is needed; according to them, testimony suffices without claim. The passage ends with a note that sighting the crescent during the day is disregarded for the past night and applies to the coming night, with Abu Yusuf's view that if seen before noon, it belongs to the past night. The tarjih (preference) in the madhhab is that sighting in one place binds all within the same matla'.

Key Terms
ثبوت الهلال (thubut al-hilal): Establishment of the sighting of the new crescent moon for Ramadan or Shawwal.
مطلع (matla'): The place of rising; a locality from which the moon is sighted.
مذهب ظاهر (madhhab zahir): The apparent or dominant position of the school as transmitted in the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They are bound by the sighting of that town.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that moon sighting in one locality binds all Muslims of the same matla', with the practical consequence that if a group fasts 29 days based on their own sighting but the moon was sighted elsewhere on the 30th, they must make up a day.
